The quality of the purging with it is that you fill the uterus or bladder with a medicine which should be heated according to the tolerance of the sick person: and be careful not for it to be too hot or too cold.
Then the sick person lies on his back and raises his legs to higher positions: and place under him a cloth or skin on which people lie.
Then anoint the end of the purgative cord with oil, or with egg white, or with mucilage from fenugreek, or similar substances. Then insert it into the anus gently. Then receive the person holding the uterus with both hands and squeeze it vigorously until all that is in the purgative cord is expelled into the intestines.
Then remove the purgative cord, and the sick person raises his legs to a wall.
If the medicine forces him out, then hold as much as possible. And if he sleeps with her all night, it is more the last resort in treatment.
If you enema him in diseases of the belly with the solution, and he hastens its departure; then repeat the enema until he is cured.
If there are hemorrhoids or sores in the anus, then let the enema be very subtle, soft, from enemas that children are given.
And this is the form of the subtle enema on which the skin is tightened.
Chapter 84.
I have said in the division on the treatment of wounds that it belongs to a book, and I will now say here the treatment of them with hands, and with medications according to completeness.
Therefore, wounds are distinguished according to the cause by which they occur, and according to the place on which the wound falls.
Therefore, as to the causes of wounds, they are like a blow from a stone; by cutting with a sword or knife; by thrusting with a spear, wood, or arrow; and similar things made of many other objects.
Wounds that occur according to the body's locations are, for example, a wound that falls on the head, neck, chest, abdomen, or back; and similar ones from those members.
And I am telling the treatment of some wounds, in order to set forth reasoning and rule for the rest of the wounds.
And I will begin with simple head wounds, since the memory of compound wounds will come at the beginning of the third chapter.
Therefore, (let us speak about) the treatment for simple head wounds If a simple wound occurs on the head and there is no bone fracture; consider then if it was caused by a blow from a stone or similar objects, and the skin has been broken but not deeply; and if the wound is large, and you fear that warm pus may develop; then proceed quickly and perform a phlebotomy of the head immediately, without delay; and extract from his blood according to its strength; if there is no prohibition against phlebotomy, particularly if the blood flowing from the wound is not much. And let phlebotomy be from the opposite side of the wounded part.
And let phlebotomy be from the opposite side of the wounded part. Let the sick take care to abstain from overfilling himself, and from wine, and heavy foods.
If something forbids a decrease, then let it be decreased by food, not approaching meats or wine.
And place over the wound if it happens to him an abscess that is hot, hot broth infused in rose oil alone, or with wine in which there is a styptic.
If you are free from a hot abscess, then place over the wound if it is recent with its own blood and has not changed the air around it; but if the air around it has already changed, then place some ointments on it until it heals.
If the wound is large or from a sword cut and similar: let not the lips of it be gathered together by small stitches; but rather gather them with suture as I will tell you about in wounds of the abdomen regarding their sutures.
If the wound has been found to be from bone and hangs, and there is no holding of it, except with a small sling: then cut it. Then care for the wound with medicines, and restore hard flesh to it where the skin is, and tell the sick to eat foods with thickness, such as heads and cooked wheat until it becomes soft; and similar things.
If you find corruption of the skin and it does not stick to the bone: then cut the corrupted part completely, and throw it away; then take care of that.
If in these wounds there is an artery or vein from which blood flows and it cannot be cut by medicines: then look for the artery above it. If you find it uncut, then cut it with a spatula or bind it. If necessity compels and it does not meet what was said, then cauterize [the wound], until the blood is separated.
If these wounds are small and simple, the matter is easy and light: thus that powder should be sprinkled over them, because it consolidates them before they change to air.
Method of the Powder for Consolidation. Take one part of olibanum, two parts of dragon's blood, and three parts of unburnt lime: grind it all very well, sieve it: fill the wound with it and compress it well, until it adheres well to the top, and coagulate it with blood.
Then compress it on top with some powder, and let it be, do not loosen it until it remains, so that it does not come apart. For the medicine is not to be removed from it until it is healed.
And sometimes lime alone does this, when you do not have olibanum and dragon's blood at hand, and it performs the same operation in many large wounds, when it is placed on them and compressed, and the wounds are recent with their own blood, which has not been altered by air.
If air has already altered it in some way, particularly when it is in the summertime, then this powder's purpose is not suitable: cure it with what was said, that is to say, place some digestive ointment on it, or place on it poultices made from barley flour, boiled with water and honey, until pus is emitted. Then cure the rest of it with the remaining treatment, until they are healed.
If it happens with a fractured wound in the bone, and it is small, then extract that with a probe, and I will now say the treatment for fractures of the skull, which will follow.
On wounds of the neck.
If a wound occurs on the neck, there is no difference in its treatment compared to a simple wound of the head.
If the nerve or artery of the neck is already cut, then if it is a nerve, there is no special skill in its treatment except to restrain its violent swelling as with scammony and helebore, and similar remedies. Since nerves are harmful and contract them.
And do not place anything cold at all over the wound: because the substance of nerves is cold and their continuation is with the brain, which is superior to the organs; let that which is used for treatment be made from soft medicines such as lime with sweet water infused several times with oil, or with rose oil, and turpentine, when it is washed even with sweet water; and other mineral stones in this manner, and moist ointments.
If the wound is large, then administer suture or raw linen strips with cotton wool. Then care for it until it heals.
If the wound is deep or a cavity forms at its lower part where pus has already gathered, then perforate it in a more depressed place.
If the artery is already cut in the wound and there is a flow of blood from it: then separate it or tie it off; or cauterize it if necessary.
If the windpipe (spondiles) is already perforated or completely severed and the patient has survived: then apply raw skin flaps with sutures over a reed for the throat, and do not touch the throat, but rather anoint it and reduce it to its natural shape. Then tie the wound with a suitable suture and let it heal for some days. Then treat the wound with ointments containing astringents and drying agents until it heals.
If the wound has already made an impression on the bone, then examine it; if there are fragments of bone within, remove all of them or as many as possible; and what cannot be prepared, let that alone until the wound decomposes, separates and the discharge subsides.
If the wound is recent, then sprinkle powder on it as I have mentioned earlier. If there appears to be bone in the wound and a fistula forms: then treat it as mentioned in its own chapter.
Chapter on wounds treated with spatulas.
If it is a wound from a lance or knife and you can see its depth, then examine it. If air is escaping from the wound when the person breathes weakly, then you know that the wound is mortal.
If there is no depth to the wound and it is recent with blood, then do not apply powder at first nor tie it up to prevent the blood from accumulating in its depth and causing death; but put on an attractive ointment and let it dissolve twice a day.
If you do not have ointment at hand, then place a piece of old cloth on the wound opening to dry what is coming out of it from moisture; and make the patient rest over the wound so that what accumulates in it may run off.
If three or more days have passed since the wound occurred and there is no spasm, no bad heart tremor, no shortness of breath, and the remaining signs are good, then you know that the wound is healed. Care for it (the patient) yourself and with liniments, and the rest of the treatment until it (the ailment) is healed.
If healing of it (the wound) has been cut off and an opening has now appeared, then you shall know that it has become a Fistula (an abnormal passageway between two organs or between the surface of the body and an organ). Treat it from its own place (the location where it is situated).
And if the wound is from sword or knife cut and it is simple on the surface of chest or back, then treat it with the procedure that has come before (which may be suturing or applying powder) depending on its size: either with suturing if large, or with powder if small.
If it has already made an impression on the bone and has cut out fragments from it: then examine the wound and hurry to extract those fragments if they are separated. If they are not separated, then leave them until the wound rots; for it is dangerous to extract them (the fragments).
As for other wounds occurring on the remaining limbs, judgment in their treatment is that which we have said (i.e., proper care and healing process).
Chapter on abdominal wounds and the outlet of the intestine, and their removal.
The rupture that occurs in the abdomen is great, medium, or small.
From a great rupture, sometimes the intestine or number of intestines comes out.
Therefore, insertion and suturing of them (the intestines) is necessary, and the suturing is more vigorous and difficult.
A small rupture is also difficult in another way. Therefore, it follows that the reduction of them (the intestines) is more difficult if you do not hurry in their insertion to their place; because it swells, opens, and becomes larger; which makes the insertion difficult.
Therefore, it is better to make a median dissection, because with it the reduction of the intestine is not difficult, as it is in these two types.
Know that the suture of the vein is according to four modes.
Two common sutures meet in the suture of the abdomen, and in the suture of other wounds of the body: and two other sutures communicate specifically with the remaining wounds. However, they are more suitable for the suture of the abdomen: and I will tell you each one with its explanation and clarification.
Therefore, I say that when a wound is small and something comes out from the intestine, it is difficult to reduce it; then this is due to one of two modes. Or because of the smallness of the dissection, as we said before; or because there is inflation in the intestine due to cold air.
When it is like this, then it is necessary to heat that part, so that you immerse a sponge or damp cloth in warm water only. Or cook in water soaked with squill, cumin, spike and myrrh, and the intestine until the inflation is resolved.
Sometimes it does wine in which there is antsickness; and this is better than water alone in resolving the inflation.
When you see that the inflation has already been resolved, then moisten the intestine with water in which artemisia or mallow has been boiled. For this reason, it relieves the entrance of it with a small amount.
If, however, its return is expelled after this treatment, then seal the dissection with too little, with the instrument with which hemorrhoids are cut.
And the first treatment is better when it is prepared, than the section; and no pressure to cut should be forced after necessity and lack of instrument.
The part of it is twisted, sharp, and another part of it not sharp, and the tip should not be subtle like a needlepoint, but rather slightly blunt: and this resembles a cat-o'-nine-tails, as you see.
When the wound is enlarged and enters the intestine, then it should be reduced to its natural shape in its own place, if possible on top of it: since it is better.
But when the rupture is wide at the lower part of the abdomen: then one should make the patient lie on his back and place his legs higher than his head. And if it is in the upper part of the abdomen: then one should raise the patient's head and chest higher than his lower parts.
And similarly, if there is a rupture in one of the two parts of the abdomen: then direct your attention and thought always to this, so that the part where the wound is should be higher than the other part.
And thus one must introduce this into large wounds and medium-sized wounds.
In small wounds: place it according to what is prepared for you.
Then make a devoted servant be present among your hands, who holds the entire rupture with his hand and presses the lips together. Then take from that which follows a thread for sewing, and this is the common method of sewing for one.
And it is to receive a needle or number of needles according to the width of the wound. Then from the end of the rupture take a quantity equal to a finger, that is, a physician's finger: and pierce the needle once through the skin at two ends, with two ends of the thread from underneath the skin on both sides until it passes through from the other side, and you have already pressed the edges of the skin together and the edges of the sifac: and four layers are formed.
Then sew with double thread around and insert a needle repeatedly from both sides until the two lips of the wound are properly gathered together by appropriate approximation.
Then release the thickness of the finger and insert another needle; next draw it straight with thread, as you did with the first needle. Therefore do not stop doing that with the necessary number of needles until you complete the entire closure of the wound.
Let the needles be of medium size, since thin needles penetrate the flesh quickly, and thick ones have a difficult entrance into the skin; therefore they should be medium-sized in both fineness and thickness.
Let the needle not be sharpened at both ends of the skin close together; in order that the flesh is not pierced quickly and the wound does not open before it has consolidated, nor do the sutures lengthen, thus preventing the wound from healing.
Then cut the end of the needle so as not to harm the weak while he is asleep; and put pieces of cloth on both sides that hold the ends of the needles, and leave them until you know that the wound has healed.
This kind of suture with such needles is more suitable for small wounds. Because it is sufficient to sew them with one or two needles, or about the same number.
The second kind of suture is common for gathering together the edges with sutures four: four skin edges and two fat layers in place of one, with a needle containing an equal amount of thread in both fineness and thickness.
Then when you penetrate with the needle these four edges, pull back the needle from the side where it entered so that the thread from above falls out of the wound; thus making the suture like a seam in cloth, and place between each stitch an amount of thickness equal to a finger's width.
Men know this stitch.
And with this stitch, I myself sewed the wound of a man in his belly, which had been wounded by a knife, and the wound was larger than one palm; and there had come out from the intestines about two palms from the middle intestine, and the rupture was in the middle of the abdomen.
I therefore brought it back after the intestine of his stayed outside the wound for twenty-four hours.
The wound was consolidated in the space which had been about fifteen days; and I cured him, and he recovered; and he lived afterwards for many years, converted to his customs.
And physicians judged that it would not heal.
And it was a miracle that I did not cure it with an ointment. Because I was in a place where no ointment could be found nor anything from the drugs. I therefore put on the wound an old loaf of bread twice a day, and I washed it assiduously with water and honey until it healed.
I will tell of two kinds of stitches according to the account of speech.
And it is that you start with an acute stitch; and you insert the needle from the outside to the inside part.
When the needle goes through the skin and along the length and width of the whole body: release the end of the sinew at the side into which you inserted the needle, and let it penetrate to the end at the other side from the inside to the outside in the opposite side.
When you have made it penetrate, then make it penetrate in the same way at the same end but in the opposite side: and release the end of the sinew which is on that side, and let the needle penetrate to the end at the other side from the inside to the outside; and make it penetrate with your penetration, as you are making it penetrate into the end of the sinew, which is in that part, until it penetrates into him completely.
Then begin also from this same side, and remove it together with the extremity that is from the sifac on the other side, and extract the point that pierced through the skin.
Return it to the same skin, and remove it along with its extremity, which is on the other side of sifac, together with this extremity: extract it from the skin that is on the other side, and do so alternately, and in reverse order until you have removed all of it according to the example given.
And the sum of this suture is that you should sew the skin that encloses the skins, so that you sew together one edge of the skin with one extremity of the skin: and that you leave it on one side from the other, until you have completed it.
The second kind of surgery is the proprietary suture as Galen said.
This discourse is told to you in the manner of those who heal like this: that you bring together all parts to correspond with each other by nature; and apply the extremity of sifac to its other extremity, and the extremity of mirach to the other extremity of it. And this is done as I have told you.
It is necessary that you point the point to the extremity of mirach close to you from the outer part, and let it penetrate into the interior of it; and leave two extremities of sifac. Then turn the point, and let it penetrate from the outer part to the interior through both extremities of sifac, that is on each side.
Then turn both of them, and let both penetrate from the interior to the exterior through two extremities of mirach, which are on the opposite side.
And this method of surgery is better than the common and easy suture, and it is the suture which has been mentioned before.
This is thus the saying of Galen also.
And certain ones said. The stitching of the abdomen should be smoothed with two points. What is to be done is that you insert one thread into both: and begin with the introduction of the needle from the place that is with you, and make it pass through until the other side, according to the stitching of those who sew leather equally.
And know that when there is a rupture in the middle of the abdomen: then the stitching of it is more difficult than the rest of the places of the abdomen.
The judgment of the medicine for a wound is the judgment of other wounds.
What is to be done is that when you take a new wound with its blood before it changes with air, and reduce the intestines, and wash it, and fit it: then place it on top of powdered meat.
If air has already changed the wound: then place on it some ointments that are present with you, which make pus, and let them drop, and consolidate them with wax, and apply it. Then care for it thus as the care of other wounds, until it is healed.
If there are no medications present with you: then place on it immediately, when it begins to emit pus, old bread: and change it twice a day, as I have taught you, until it is healed.
For you do not need any other medicine for multiple compositions, when the wounds are simple.
If you fear that the wound communicates with the principal limbs in injury, then it is necessary that you immerse wool softened in warm oil or rose oil: and place it in the surrounding area of places that are between the groins and under the armpits.
If he feels pain or punctures or putrefaction in his intestines (for it often happens), then clisterize him with warm wine of pitch, black: especially if the putrefaction has already reached the intestine and an exit has been made penetrating into its concavity.
And know that what is from the large intestine is easier to heal; and what is from the small intestines is more difficult to heal.
The intestine that is called Jejunum does not accept healing from a wound that occurs in it at all: and this is because of the multitude of its veins, and the size of them, and because of the subtlety of its body, and because of the proximity of its nature to the nerves.
If what comes out from the wound is greenish and you seize it quickly: return it as you would return the intestines equally.
But if it has passed time and already become black or greenish: then you must tie it with thread above the place that has turned black from it, so that there is no bleeding. For in the greenish there are veins and arteries.
Then cut what is beyond that ligament and put two ends of hanging thread from the lower part of the wound outside it: to relieve you from the attraction of it and the extraction of the greenish at the place of the wound, and the discharge of pus from the wound.
Chapter on the wound that occurs in the intestine.
When there is a rupture in the intestine and it is small: then it is possible for it to be restored in some men: therefore, because I have seen a man who was wounded in his stomach with the thrust of a lance, and the wound was on the right side of the stomach, and the wound was old; and a fistula has been formed, and discharge and gas have come out from it.
I therefore gave treatment, until I was not confident in its healing; neither did I cease to examine it, until it was healed, and the place was consolidated.
Once I saw the place already consolidated, I feared an accident happening to the sick one in its belly; and no such accident happened to it from that source. And it remained in better conditions for itself, eating and drinking, urinating, and taking a bath, and exercised in its regimen.
And some of the experimenters said that when a wound occurs in the intestine and it is small, then it should be tied thus. And there is, to take large-headed ants. Then gather two lips of the wound, and place one ant with an open mouth on both lips of the wound. When the ant's head is upon them, and it closes its mouth, cut off its head, for it will stick, and will not be loosened.
Then place another ant near the first one, and do not cease to make this with ants according to the size of the wound in total.
Then reduce it (the wound), and let it heal. For those heads remain attached in the intestine until it is eaten (the intestine), and healed, and no harm comes to the sick one deeply.
And it is now possible to tie the intestine even with a fine thread; this has been removed from the animal's intestine, attached with it when it is introduced into the needle.
And there is, to take the end of this thread from the intestine, and to be scraped off much. Then tie a fine linen thread at its end. Then introduce the thread into the needle, and let it with the thread go through the intestine. Then reduce it to dissection.
This method of suturing with worms and intestines is only by way of trust and hope.
Know that a large wound, broad, especially if it is one of the thin intestines: then skill is not ingenuity but subtlety, and there is no healing at all.
Chapter on the Care of Sores and Fistulas.
Know that every wound or boil, when it becomes old and decays, and turns into an ulcer and does not heal, and always discharges pus and does not abscess: then it is universally named in whatever member it is found a Fistula, and we call it Zuchemen.
Indeed, a Fistula is correctly called a drained abscess, hard, white; without pain, having the shape of a bird's feather beak: and for that reason some call it Risatus, that is, beak-shaped.
And at times it is moist, which discharges pus. And perhaps the moisture is divided at certain times. And sometimes this moisture is abundant, and it is scarce; and it is thick, and it is thin.
And now I have already divided the wounds, when I said an ulcer that does not heal, does not produce new flesh in it, is not that except for one of nine causes.
Of which one is, either because of the scarcity of blood in the body; or because of its malice; or because hard flesh is between it and above its surface, preventing the growth of new flesh; or because there is much dirt and corruption; or because the ulcer itself is putrid, and the matter that is discharged from it is of poor quality; or because the treatment is unsuitable for its cure; or because falling decay in the region is a kind of plague, as happens in Caesaraugusta: where the digestion of diseases becomes difficult, and the healing of boils is delayed by nature; or because there is one bone, or a number of bones in it.
And now I have shown all these causes and their signs, and the cures for them with the medicines in the division; and here it is necessary that I make you know the cure for a Fistula (or Zuchemen), which is done by the way of operation with the hand.
Know, therefore, that a Fistula occurs in all members of the body. For these are Fistulas that reach the great veins and arteries, or nerves, or tendons; or the intestine, or bladder, or some of the ribs; or some of the vertebrae in the back, or some of the joints composed of several bones, such as hands, feet: and there are those that approach the proximity of the main member and similar ones.
That which is this Fistula according to this manner, is difficult for healing, and does not receive treatment, and the occupation with it is labor and ignorance.
If it is some of the Fistulas whose depth does not reach to those places that I mentioned and you hope and trust in its treatment, and wish to come to knowledge of it, then take a probe made of copper or iron, if the Fistula points in a straight direction, and search for it with the same.
If, however, there is a twist in the Fistula, then investigate it with a probe made of fine lead. Because lead, due to its softness, yields and bends around the twist.
If the Fistula has many openings, it is not possible for you to sign upon it with a probe. Cauterize therefore, from among its openings, one opening.
Because the moisture with which you cauterize it, flows away towards other openings and runs out of them.
Then examine and inquire according to the manner that is possible for you: so that you know whether there is a bone or nerve, and the Fistula has a deep hollow, far away or near; or if it is a single Fistula with many openings, standing on all of it, with your utmost strength: with your questioning, which you ask of the sick person, and by feeling for pain in compression with your hand upon the place, and similar signs and causes of injury.
When therefore you have stood over all those matters in the correct position, then proceed to treatment according to confidence: and it is that you should consider then, if it is a Fistula nearby appearing, or in a safe place distant from a joint, or from a nerve, or an artery, or vein, or one of the places which I told you: then cut the Fistula according to what was stated in my narrative, and remove what is in it from filtration, and corrupted flesh, what is born in its opening from flesh or ulcer, and similar things: and take care of it until it heals.
If the Fistula is deep, and it is as stated: then it is necessary to cut it deeply as much as possible for you. Then clean it from all corrupted flesh of it.
Then administer a linen cloth dipped in sharp medicines: and insert it up to the hollow of the Fistula which you have grasped with iron: do this several times, until the sharp medicine corrodes all that remains in the hollow of the Fistula due to corruption.
Then restore it with ointments which generate healthy flesh, until it heals.
If it does not heal through this, then cauterize it according to what was stated before.
If the cause of the Fistula is bone, and you are sure about it: then investigate it, and cut it according to what I told you: if it does not prohibit you from anything from a vein, or nerve, or major member, as was said before.
If a bone is exposed to you, and there is something corrupted and black in it: then file it until the corruption is completely removed. Then take care of it with that which causes it to heal, until it heals.
If it does not heal and discharges pus as it was doing: then you know, that you have not reached complete eradication of the corruption.
Excise it again on the second occasion, and investigate its cause and cleanse it with your utmost strength.
Then restore it. If then it heals, that is good; and if not, then you know that the corruption is deep within the body, which you have not been able to reach. You have no skill in that matter, except to let it be by nature.
If a small bone is corrupted and it is possible for you, pull it out with delicate forceps that fit the case.
If there are many bones, then investigate how to pull out all of them and do not leave anything behind with your strength.
If however it presents an obstacle to you from some moisture, and there is no fear or error in the incision: then cut those moist areas and flesh, if they are present, and release the bones.
If however you are not prepared to remove the bones at your time, then place something on the wound that will rot it, and let it go until it rots due to the flesh and moisture around those bones.
And keep the wound so that it does not heal. And tighten the incision that you made during your operation. Rather place on it if you fear it, a piece of charcoal softened with ground sulfur and oil; or a piece of charcoal soaked in Egyptian ointment, or any green ointments.
For when those moist areas rot and separate the bones, cleaning them away will be easier, and pulling out the bones themselves will be facilitated.
If there is a large bone, such as the thighbone or hip bone, and what is corrupted from it is a major part: then shave it with a final file until the blackness and corruption is removed. Then restore the wound.
If what is within it (the bone) has decayed and a large part is already rotten to the marrow of the bone, then it is necessary to saw that (bone) and cut through it all to the place where the decay has reached: then treat it until it heals.
And I tell you this secret, which happens to a man in his leg: place it as an example and aid for your operation.
A young man of about thirty years had pain in his leg, caused by something that moved upon him within the body (from inside), until it reached the leg and an abscess was formed with a large size: and there was no external cause for him.
Time passed while the man was in error with the physicians until the abscess was opened, and much matter gathered around him. And the treatment of his leg was delayed until it became fistulous and many openings were made in it, from which pus and bodily moisture flowed out.
Many physicians treated him for about two years and there was no skilled hand in him until I was called, and saw his leg, and the matter that flowed from those openings with a great flow: even the whole body of the man was already rotten and his skin had become yellowish.
I inserted a probe into one of the openings, and the probe reached the bone.
Then I examined all the openings: and found them to reach each other from all parts of the leg.
I approached and sawed through one of the openings until I exposed part of the bone: and I found it rotten; which had already been corroded, decayed, decomposed, perforated until it had penetrated to the marrow.
Prepare therefore what has been detected for me, and prepare it from corrupted bone.
And I ordered that it should not be on the bone unless that corruption, which had been removed, was to be sawed off. And I had already uprooted it.
Then I placed the wound's restoration with strengthening medicines at a long distance. And it is not strengthened.
Then I tried again and probed over the bone a second time above the first detection; and I found continuous corruption with the bone; and I sawed off what appeared to me also from that corruption.
Then I attempted its restoration. And it is not restored.
I then probed it again and did not cease to cut the bone in parts, trying for its restoration; and it was not restored until I had cut out a palm's breadth from the bone, and extracted that along with its marrow.
Then I restored it with medicines, and it was quickly strengthened and healed; and this repetition in the operation and section of it was not necessary except because of the arrangement of weakness, the frailty of the patient, his poor tolerance, and my fear of death for him; since he often fell into syncopal evacuations during all hours on account of excessive bleeding.
Thus he was completely healed; and a hard piece of meat grew at the place of the bone, and it turned out well for his body, and his strength returned to him; and he regained his constitution, and he suffered no harm from walking at all.
If however the prominent bone at a place in the body is already fractured, then it must be sawed off in this manner: and that is, to take a ligament, tie it at the end of the protruding bone; and command someone to extend it upward; and place another thicker cord from wool first. Then bind that [the wound] on the bone which is flesh: and extend its end, so that you draw the flesh towards the lower parts: and you expose the flesh over the place where you wish to cut with the saw, so that the saw does not cut into the flesh.
Place a stick or a board under the bone below properly: because when you do that, it is not forbidden to cut rotten flesh.
And it is necessary that the saw be above the rotten place a little bit, so that there is no rotting in the hollow of the bone: and it does not appear to the sense in its appearance, and compel another to serve in its place.
If the bone is rotten and not prominent, on the contrary it is joined to another part: and the rotting is in the middle of it or in a part of it: then expose the flesh from all parts completely. Then place a stick below.
Afterwards saw from the first side where there is rotting, until the saw is joined to another part: and let the saw be according to its length a little bit away from the rotting, as we said.
If the rotting is at a joint, cut the joint which is rotten and saw the bone where it is joined from another part.
But if the rotting is in the continuity of two joints, then there is no skill in that except by abrasion.
If the rotting is in the beak of the hand or foot: then the matter is very difficult in that. But it is necessary to examine how the rotting appears to you. And abrade it, and cleanse it according to whatever position is possible for you, and with whatever skill it can be corrected for you: when it does not create opposition for you from the Vein or Nerve.
And know that there are many saws and knives for cutting these bones, according to the position of the bones, their preparation, their thickness, their subtlety, their size, their smallness or greatness, their hardness, and their rarity. Therefore, it is necessary that one prepares a suitable instrument for every kind of operation.
And know this: these operations indicate to you the type of instrument required of you, when your study is prolonged. And knowledge of the methods of this art is evidence of the methods of diseases; then you will find for yourself that which suits it among the instruments for every illness.
And I am confirming for you at the end of this chapter the number of instruments, which you will place as examples on which to cut, and proof of the test on others.
Form of another Saw.
Form of a Scissors for Joints. The head of this Scissors should be like the head of a star-shaped nail; and its points should be like those of an elongated fork.
It is not suitable unless it rubs against the heads of the joints when they are broken, or a large and thick bone.
Form of a Larger Scissors.
Form of a Wide Scissors.
Form of a Scissors with a hollow inside.
Form of another Fine Scissors.
Form of another Scissors with a curved end.
Form of a Small and Fine Scissors with a pointed tip.
This resembles a Saw, and its end is like a Razor: it is used in many places due to the reason of bones.
The shape that suits for cutting through bone is similar to that of a Razor.
Its end is triangular, and its edges are sharp.
It is made from India. And similarly, everything that we have said should be made of Razors, Knives, and Saws.
Chapter on the incision of extremities, and the sawing of bones.
Rotten extremities are either due to an external cause, or an internal one.
When you have treated the corruption with medicines and it does not improve, and you see that the corruption is spreading into a limb which has not been affected: then it is necessary to amputate that limb up to where the corruption extends, so that it may pass through it from death, or from some greater evil due to the loss of the member.
And the sign that this happens is when the limb turns black, until it is thought to be dead; or when fire has burned it, or it has rotted after turning black: until the rot spreads up to where it follows the limb, and it creeps through the whole body.
Therefore, when making the incision and similarly if the cause of corruption is due to the bite of some venomous creatures such as marine scorpions, weasels, adders, and similar ones.
If corruption or a bite occurs on the extremity of a finger, then amputate the finger and do not leave space for the rot to spread, so that it may creep up to the rest of the hand.
And similarly if it happens in the hand, then amputate it at the root of the wrist and do not leave space for the rot to spread, until it reaches the arm bone.
And if it invades him (the wound), then cut the arm at the elbow joint.
If the corruption goes through about the elbow: then there is death for the weak man (patient). And what is given beyond that for treatment, is the amount of virtue.
And likewise do the same with the foot, when the corruption invades the toe; then cut it at one of the joints (toes); and if it reaches the front part of the foot, then cut off the whole foot. And if it goes up to the knee, then cut the leg at the joint of the knee.
But if the corruption has already come above the knee, then there is no skill (remedy) in it, except to release and abandon the weak man to death.
And the method of cutting (amputating) a member or binding it is that you tighten the ligament below the place where you wish to cut and tighten another ligament above. And extend one minister (attendant) to below, and extend another attendant ligament upward. You separate the flesh that is between two ligaments with a broad spatula until the whole flesh is exposed. Then it is cut or bound (amputated).
And it is necessary that you put a linen cloth on all sides (the wound), and the saw must not touch healthy parts; and the weak man suffers additional injury, and hot abscess.
And if there is bleeding internally while you are working, then quickly cauterize the place or put some absorbing powders on it to stop the bleeding. Then return to your treatment until it is complete.
Then bind the wounded limb with an appropriate ligament and care for it until it heals.
And I tell you what happens to a man in his foot; this is the same thing that I tell you (happens).
What happens is that a blackening occurs in his foot due to a burning similar to fire combustion; and the corruption primarily begins with his toe until it affects the entire foot.
He fell sick from himself, after observing that the corruption was happening in a limb with intense pain which he perceived from the pain, and due to his burning; and he wounded himself at the junction, and he was cured.
And after a long period of time had passed, the same corruption occurred in the index finger of his own hand.
Therefore he came to me and I attempted to recover the loss with him by placing medicines over his hand after cleansing his body; but the loss was not recovered, and it started spreading to another finger until the corruption began affecting the whole hand.
He then called for me to cut off his hand; but I refused, hoping for a recovery of the loss from my side; and I also feared death due to the cutting of his hand. Because the man's strength was above his fall.
And when he had given up hope on me, he returned to his own land; but then he came back to me because he fell sick and cut off his whole hand; and he was cured.
I did not bring this narrative except as help for that which falls under the type of this illness; and as a signification to indicate how it should be handled.
Section on the care of sinuses, and the quality of purgatives with medicines.
When an apostema occurs in some fleshy parts and the length of the apostema continues until it causes collection of pus; then it bursts or is perforated, and what is inside it exits through the pus, leaving a vacant place as if it were a vessel; and the skin that is over it becomes thin like a cloth that has been woven; and it does not come to an end in its corruption, nor press on the bone, nor the nerve, nor the ligament; it deserves to be named Sinus; and it is not called Fistula; however if the length of it continues until the corruption affects one of these organs, then it is called Fistula or ulcer.
The care of it is that you should cut the entire skin as rolled [over]; and especially when it has been finely minced and made like cloth; and trust that it will not adhere to the place because of its corruption.
If you expect the skin to stick, and this does not follow from its corruption: and if there is a thickening of the flesh in the skin, then care for it with Clisterium: and it is that you should consider towards the Sinew.
Then if it is large and there is pus that is emitted from it which has a foul smell: then clisterize it with the Egyptian medicine, which is vinegar, oil, and honey, parts receiving equal amounts, to be mixed in a vessel: and boil it over fire until the medicine turns red: and may it become of the consistency of honey.
Then take from it what is necessary for you: and dissolve it with water and honey, and clisterize the Sinew with it: and tie up its opening, and let the medicine remain in it for the amount of two hours.
Then extract it with pressure: do this for days until the Sinew is purified and the foul smell is removed.
And sometimes it is clisterized with water and ashes when this medicine is not present for you: and it is that you should take ashes of vine wood, or ashes from oak trees: and throw water upon it. Then boil it, and clisterize with it until you are confident that the Sinew has been washed clean.
If the weak patient is not able to tolerate the Egyptian medicine or water and ashes, then clisterize it with water and honey, into which something of crushed honey has already been mixed. Or clisterize it with honey and wine mixed, since the property of honey is to purify and wash: and wine makes the Sinew stick, especially when there is an excess of stiffness and dryness in the wine.
Whenever you perform that [procedure], and believe that the corruption of the sinus has already been removed: then purge it with an enema, together with what causes flesh to grow therein.
And [it is] to receive from the plaster dyafinicon, and to dissolve it with rose oil and stiptic wine, or to purge it with certain medicines and other ointments, whose properties I have written about in the treatise on ointments.
If the opening of the sinus is narrow, and the enema cannot enter through it: then enlarge it slightly with an iron; or place linen wrapped in Egyptian ointment, or Basilicon ointment inside until it becomes wider.
And similarly, if the opening is also wide, then gather its lips with a suture; and allow the enema to enter the quantity to which it can enter without being added to.
And similarly, if the opening from which pus flows is elevated above: then cut it at a lower location that is within it, so that the pus may run out below; because when pus becomes obstructed in the depth of the sinus, it prevents flesh from being generated therein.
If it is not possible for you to cut the sinus near its base as you wish: then try to prepare a limb in such a way that the pus can run out from there with ease, according to how it is prepared for you. Try not to retain anything of the pus whatsoever within it; and receive consolidating medicines that are applied to the mark, suitable for what you wish. Spread them out on a linen cloth. And let the cloth be of such size that there is communication with the entire sinus. Then make an incision with forceps in a straight line on the opening of the sinus, larger than the opening of the sinus itself. Then extend from soft unguents on a different cloth, according to the size of the hole: and place it over it with long distance from the orifice Sinus, so that the discharge of pus from it is relieved: and do not remove the large cloth, unless after several days instead.
The one who is over the orifice of Sinus, that is the one you should remove at every hour, so that what accumulates in it due to pus may come out: and so that you know whether what is flowing from it is much, little, digested, or undigested.
And when you have again examined the place of Sinus itself, whether it feels pain to its master or not, and whether there is pain in it, and if there is an abscess in it, or not.
When therefore your treatment is so extended, and you see the matter which comes out on the orifice of Sinus, then you will know that Sinus has already adhered or approaches adhesion: then soluble it in the morning, or in the third day: and when you dissolve the wound, then change the cloth, and renew the unguent.
If however pus flows out of Sinus after a long time, then do not despair of its adhesion, for healing is approaching, since there are people who delay the generation of flesh in their wounds, and from them there are those who act contrary to this.
If however pus flows out of Sinus for many days undigested, then you will know that Sinus is not adhering.
If therefore adhesion of Sinus is delayed and prolonged: then increase in the drying treatment, but let the physicians make sure that the medicines are moist in substance and dry in effectiveness, like unguentum dyafinicon, when excess addition from zegi is added to it.
And from the last (step) which is treated, it is, that you receive an ointment called dyafinicon, and it should be dissolved with rose oil, and it should be rubbed over it an equal amount of ancient wine in substance.
Then mix (it) with that, and use (it).
And now it makes a similar action when you do not have the ointment dyafinicon, honey, when you cook it until it thickens, and you use it. Or receive myrrh, aloe, and frankincense: grind everything, and powder it over honey while it is over the fire. Then spread linen over it (the wound), and sew it over a bladder: and spread the honey that you cooked, until it thickens, and powder its species, and put it over the linen, and sew it.
And at times aristolochia longa, the roots of celestial lily, and its bulb are administered in that (situation), and wheat flour, and centaury. Use them (the simple ingredients or the combined ones), grind them, and sift them: and powder it over the honey that I mentioned, and use it.
If this (the bladder) prolongs and is not cured by this treatment: then you know that there is corruption in its depth; or it makes an impression on the bones, or on the hard members, or ligaments: then cure it with fistula treatment, according to what was mentioned earlier.
Â¶ On the care of paronychia and eroded nail, and incision of the finger added, and amputation.
Panaritium is an abscess that arises under the nail of the thumb of the hand, or foot; and perhaps it arises in other fingers.
When this (the abscess) prolongs and is neglected in its treatment, and it is opened with a hot incision: or it ruptures and releases pus, until it corrodes the root of the nail: and perhaps it ruptures it completely, and perhaps the corruption reaches the bone, until there is a foetid smell: and the tip of the finger becomes broad, and its color becomes dark.
When therefore you treat it (the nail), with what we said concerning division, and your treatment does not improve it: then it is necessary that you cut with iron the entire superfluity which remains from the nail. Then cauterize the wound after that. For cautery is very helpful (or beneficial).
If however the bone is healthy and the nail also is healthy, and empty additions of the nail already press the flesh towards the interior, and begin to prick it and wound it: then it is necessary that you place subtle radium under the angle of the nail which wounds the flesh, and push it up: and cut the flesh with ease, and place on top of what remains of the flesh from medicines which burn, corrosives, until it is completely removed. Then treat that with ointments, until it heals.
If however the corruption already presses on the bone, then it is necessary that you cut that bone and remove it: for it will never heal as long as a corrupted bone remains in it.
If you see corrosion and corruption creeping towards the finger, then cut it off at a joint, according to what was mentioned regarding its remembrance. Then treat that, until it heals.
If however the nail is rubbed or hit, and pain occurs which is very strong, then it is necessary that you use a leech first. Then cut the nail with a sharp spatula cut from above to below.
And be careful that the cut does not reach the flesh which is under the nail, for you will cause severe pain to occur below it, and this will be a cause of the addition of new flesh in the place.
Then treat the place with what soothes the pains.
The finger which is born in some human hands, perhaps it is entirely of flesh: and perhaps there are bones in some of them, and perhaps there is a nail in it: and some of them are born at the joint of the root, or the birthplace of one of them is in some of the joints of the finger. The one who is born with knots on a finger, does not move; and the one who is born at the juncture of a finger, perhaps moves.
Therefore, for the fleshy part that is among them, its incision is easy. It is, to cut it at the root with a wide spatula.
The cure for one who is born at the juncture of the root is difficult. Therefore, refrain from cutting it.
But concerning the one who is born on a finger at the end of the knots, then you should first cut through its flesh with a round incision to the bone. Then saw the bone with one of those saws suitable for that. Then care for it until it heals.
Consolidation however that occurs in fingers, often happens either from the birth of a man, that is, being born with it, or from consolidation of a wound, or burn of fire, and similar conditions.
Therefore, it is necessary to cut off the consolidation, until the fingers return to their natural shape. Then place linen or cloth soaked in rose oil between them to prevent consolidation quickly and separate them; or place a thin lead plate between them until the location consolidates according to what is necessary.
And similarly, if consolidation occurs in some fingers with the palm, then cut off the consolidation according to what is prepared and suitable for the shape of the member.
Chapter on Incision Defects.
Chapter XCII. Uites are swollen veins, large, full of melancholic excesses that occur in many parts of the body; and their most frequent occurrence is in the legs, especially in those traveling or working, and in carriers.
It is necessary that you administer the incision of the body from black cholera several times with a strong incision.
Then, let the patient's vein be opened (phlebotomized) with the basilica vein.
The treatment of these (wounds or conditions), with iron, is according to two methods. One method is that they are opened and black blood is drawn out. A different method is, to elevate the Vein and extract it completely.
The incision of this (vein) is in accordance with this method. Pour a plentiful amount of warm water on the leg first, until the thick and foul blood is dissolved. Then bind the weakened leg from its upper part of the hip, down to its lower part of the thigh with a bandage. Then cut the vein at one, or two, or three wide sections.
Then draw blood, that is, extract blood with your hand from the lower part of the leg upwards, and it is necessary that a phlebotomist treats the weakened first. Then cut the nail down to its base until you see a quantity of blood that seems sufficient and that the strength of the weakened person can bear.
Then tie it up and order him to abstain from foods producing black bile; and repeat the purges and bloodletting when the veins are full, for that will harm the weakened one.
The bleeding of this kind is as follows: Shave the leg of the sick one, if there are many hairs on it. Then introduce him into the bath: or plunge him in his leg with hot water until it turns red and veins appear; or exercise with strong movement, if there is no bath present, until the limb warms up.
Then cut the skin along the vein in a straight line: or at the end of it near the knee, or at the lower part near the heel.
Then open the skin with scissors and scrape off the vein from every side until it is felt: and at its appearance, what you see will turn from red to darker shades. When it reaches the skin, you see it white like a cord.
Then insert the lancet beneath it until it rises and comes out of the skin: hang it with a blunt scissor.
Then make another incision near that incision with three fingers. Then scrape the skin from the upper side of the vein until it appears. Then lift it with lancets as you did before, and hang it with another scissor as you did at first.
Then make another incision or multiple incisions if needed for that purpose. Then scrape it and make the last incision at the heel.
Then pull out the vein to the incision that is above it.
And do this until you have pulled out the vein from the above incision or all the incisions, until it comes out completely; then cut it.
If it does not obey you to pull it out and lift it: then insert the sharp awl with a strong thread and tie it, and pull it out; or insert the lancet beneath it: and move your hand in every direction until it comes out. And avoid cutting (it). If it is cut, it becomes very difficult for you to drain it, and harm occurs to the patient from it.
When you remove it entirely, then place a wool soaked in wine and rose oil, or oil, over the wound site and care for (it) until it heals.
If it is a vein that is contracted, having a curvature with coiling in parts, and not as we have said according to straightness: then it is necessary for you to make an incision over all its curvatures, and the places of its coiling.
Then suspend it with forceps until you drain it entirely: and avoid cutting the vein or wounding (it) at your incision; for it will be difficult for you to drain or extract it. Therefore, conserve your caution.
The form of the incision that heals a vein.
The form of the incision for a blind vein.
It is not its coarseness like the other veins, nor is it sharp at the ends to harm the vein; and it is of a curved thickness, but lenient; because if it were subtle, the vein would be cut by its subtlety; on the contrary, let it have excessive thickness, as we said.
On the extraction of the leg vein.
This vein is generated in the legs of hot lands, as Alizegi (says), and the land of Arabs, and in dry, hot lands with little softness, that is, where no grass grows nor trees.
And perhaps it is generated in other parts of the body besides the legs.
Its generation is certainly from the putrefaction that occurs under the skin, as happens within the bodies of snakes and intestinal worms, and worms generated between the skin and flesh.
The sign of the commencement of this Vein's course is that it happens with a vehement inflammation in the leg. Then, the area is excavated. Then, the vein begins to emerge from the place of that excavation, as if it were the root of a tree or an animal.
When its end appears from there, then it is necessary to roll over the lead on top of it, whose weight should be one keil of a drachma, until two drachms and a node; and let the suspended lead go from the leg.
Whenever something comes out of it to the outside, roll that over in lead and tie it. If it extends greatly, then cut off a part of it, and roll over the rest. And do not cut it off by its root before it has come out entirely. Because if you cut it off, it contracts and goes into the flesh; it causes an abscess to appear, and pus and a bad ulcer at the place.
Therefore, it should be treated gently and pulled out gradually until it comes out entirely, and nothing remains of it in the body; and from this vein, in some people, there comes out something that is five fingers long and ten. And now it has come to me, because a man of twenty fingers has come out of it.
If it is cut during your treatment, then insert a ray into its hole and perforate it with a long hole as the body goes out until everything that is inside it from matter comes out; and apply the putrefaction of the place with medicines for some days. Then, treat it with the treatment of abscesses.
And this vein sometimes has many branches, especially when it appears at the junction of the foot, or even in the foot itself; and there are many openings for it, and a branch comes out from every opening.
Treat it accordingly, as we said in the division, and with what was previously mentioned.
On the section of worms generated beneath the skin, and it is called Bouina disease.
This disease is called Bouina disease in a certain land among us: because it often occurs to cows, and there is a small worm that is born between the skin and the flesh.
It moves throughout the entire body, ascending and descending; and it is felt by the senses during its movement from limb to limb until it bursts at any point where it bursts on the skin and comes out.
And its generation is from the decay of certain fluids. As worms and snakes, that is, earthworms and roundworms occur in the belly.
It is feared from its mischief. Because when it moves within the body and rises to the head, and arrives at a place; then perhaps it opens there and comes out, and destroys an eye; and this often happens.
Therefore, when one wishes to cure it and extract it, this is not done except during its movement and appearance to the senses.
It is necessary therefore that you tighten what is above it, and what is below it with a strong binding. Then cut above it, and extract it.
If it sinks into the flesh and you do not find it; then place a cautery on the place with fire until it dies.
And the greater danger is its corruption, which destroys an eye, as we said.
If you see it has already reached the head near the eye; then tighten under it with a good forehead binding. Then cut above it, and extract it.
The sick person should use mundification (cleansing) of his body with medications dissolving bad and corrupt humors.
Let him avoid foods causing putrefaction.
Chapter on surgery concerning Nakir's disease.
This disease is called Nakir on our land: it is a pain that occurs in some members, then changes from one member to another.
And I myself have seen this, as I am telling you. I was called to a sick woman in some villages. And she uncovered her arm, and I saw a small swelling on the vein of the arm.
After staying for an hour, I saw the swelling move with a worm-like motion, rapidly advancing to her shoulder, like quicksilver running from one place to another.
Therefore, the pain left that place and was fixed in her shoulder.
Then, it remained seated for an hour, and it ran through the rest of her body until it reached another arm.
Then she told me that it turned around in her entire body, as I saw.
I was amazed at the swiftness of its change from one member to another. And I, before this incident, had not seen this disease appear in such a manner with my eyes. Nevertheless, I have seen many who find pain changing from one member to another; but I had not seen it as in the woman.
And I do not think that it was anything other than the fact that the woman was one of those in the dry villages with detected veins; hence, this shows that the feeling of putridity had changed.
It is necessary that, according to this reason, hidden veins do not appear in those who are delicate and in moist bodies.
When you have the intention of healing it and the patient feels that pain, then if it appears to you as we said, then press above it and quickly beneath it; cut above it until that windiness contracts; and cauterize the place.
If you do not see it with your eyes: then treat it by pressing the body and what expels windiness, and crush them with fetid pills and pills of resin and similar things.
Chapter on the Extraction of Arrows.
Arrows are diverse according to their kinds and the places where they fall on the body.
Their diversity is because there are some that have large tips and small ones; concave and blunt ones. And among them are those that have three corners. And of those with tongues (barbed arrows). And of those with stars (multi-pointed arrows).
Those that are on the limbs where they fall, are according to two modes. Or limbs are from the main concave members such as brain, heart, liver, lung, kidneys, intestines, bladder and similar. When it falls into one of these members and you see signs of death that I will tell you later, then it is necessary to devote the extraction of that arrow from them, for death follows it where it is, in many dispositions. And when those signs are not visible to you and the arrow is not hidden in the depth of the member, then extract it, and care for the wound.
For the signs of the brain when an arrow falls into it and penetrates the bone and exits from what is above the brain are: because hot sweats occur from it, and blindness, and dizziness, and redness in the eyes, and swelling, and redness of the tongue, and spasm, and confusion of mind, and vomiting of cholera. And perhaps blood comes out from the nose or ears. And perhaps speech is cut off, and voice destroyed. And there comes out from the wound place whiteness like plaster. And it comes out similar to watery flesh.
If those signs are visible to you, then refrain from treatment, and extract the arrow if it does not come out.
The signs of the arrow when it falls into the heart are: that it is near the left breast, and it feels as if it were fixed in a hard thing, not in a soft one. And perhaps there is movement similar to pulse movement, and blood runs black from the wound. And this follows coldness of limbs, cold sweat, and fainting. Know therefore, that death clearly recedes.
And the sign of an arrow when it wounds the lung is the issuance of frothy blood from the wound; and the vessels following the neck should be removed, and the color [of the patient] becomes weak. And it breathes out a great, deep breath, and seeks cold air's attraction.
If the arrow falls into the pleura, which is in the chest; then it is near the small ribs; and a great panting with very intense pain, with sighing, occurs, and all the limbs of the shoulders move.
If the arrow falls into the stomach, then perhaps something undigested comes out from the wound.
If an arrow falls into the abdomen and sticks there, something like feces comes out from the wound, or pus, or already ruptured intestine. Therefore, there is no hope for its cure, nor in the removal of the arrow.
If the arrow falls into the bladder and exits urine; and something goes out from it to the outside, and the pain is greater over the weak one; then you know that it is mortal.
The rest of the limbs, such as the face, and the neck, and throat, and spatula, and assistant, and dorsal vertebrae, and furculum, and hip, and leg, and similar members are saved according to many positions; when the arrow does not find an artery or nerve, and it is not a poisoned arrow.
And I tell you that of these arrows, as a sign for your treatment.
That is, because a man had an arrow shot into his tear duct of the eye, at the root of the nose. Therefore, I removed it myself from the other side, under the ear fat of his; and he was healed, and no harm came to his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from the Jew, which fell into his eye fat beneath the lower eyelid; and already the arrow was hidden, and I did not succeed in obtaining it except for its small end, with which it adhered to its wooden shaft; and the arrow was large among the arrows of the Turks, with a soft iron tip, in which there were no ears.
The Jew was healed, and no further harm occurred to his eye.
And I extracted another arrow from the throat of a Christian.
And it was an Arabic arrow, and it had ears. I cut therefore across the middle (of the wound): and already it was sunken into his throat. So I made it thin with a file until I extracted it; and the Christian was healed.
And I extracted another arrow from a man who it had fallen into his abdomen; and we thought that he would die from it.
After he had passed thirty days and nothing changed in his condition, I made an incision over the arrow; and I opened it up, and extracted it; and he was healed, and no further harm occurred to him.
And I saw a man whose arrow had fallen into his back, and the wound had become hardened over it. After seven years, the arrow came out at the root of his hip.
I saw a woman whose arrow had fallen into her abdomen, and the wound had become hardened over it, and the arrow remained within her; and nothing changed in her condition, and the woman did not suffer any harm from her natural operations.
I saw another man whose arrow had fallen into his face; and the wound became hardened, and remained, causing him little pain; and there were many similar cases.
And I removed an arrow from a man among the princes of the King, which had fallen into his nose middle, and already it was slightly sinking towards the right side; and the entire arrow was hidden.
I was therefore called to treat him after the loss of the arrow for three days. Then I found the arrow wound very narrow.
Then I inserted it with a fine probe and did not feel it; but himself indeed discovered a puncture and pain on his right ear side. Therefore I hoped that this puncture was from the end of the arrow.
I therefore anointed the place with an ointment containing attracting and digestive properties, confident that the wound would heal and reveal the sign of the arrow so that I could cut above it; but this did not happen in the wound which indicated that the arrow had reached its destination.
I therefore attended him with the ointment for many days; but this did not happen as expected, and the wound healed meanwhile; and he remained weak from the extraction of it until he felt the arrow one day within his nose. And he told me that.
And he told me that. I therefore put strong and corrosive medicine on that wound for many days until it opened; and when I probed it with a fine probe, I felt the tip of the arrow which was attached to wood.
Then I added the same strong and corrosive medicine to the opening of the wound until we could see clearly the tip of the arrow; and he passed away with me after an interval of about four months.
Then when the wound had healed and I was able to insert forceps into it and move it without resistance, I did not cease to examine it and try various means until on one day I began with well-made forceps, according to the shape described at the end of the chapter, until I extracted it. Then I restored the wound.
And indeed physicians judge that the cartilage of the nose cannot be restored. I therefore restored it [the wound], and the wound was healed, and the patient recovered completely in good health: nothing at all harmed him.
And I tell you the quality of the extraction of certain arrows: so that you may set down the rule and significance on what I have not said.
Since the parts of this art and their distinctions are not comprehended with the narrative; nor does the book contain it, and a skillful craftsman compares little with much; and with what is present, regarding what is absent; and he discovers a new operation, and a new instrument for descending foreign matters of this art when they occur.
Therefore, I say that arrows do not exit from the members to which they are fixed except in two ways. Or through the attraction of the place from which it entered. Or, on the contrary, by the part opposite [the one] where it entered.
And that which exits from where it entered is either a bullet proceeding into a fleshy place; and it is attracted and exits.
If it does not obey to exit from the side where it fell: then it is necessary that you leave it for some days, until the flesh surrounding it becomes putrid; and its attraction subsides, and its exit occurs.
And similarly, if it is fixed in bone, and does not obey to your effort to make it exit; then leave it for some days also; and move it every day with its attraction and motion until it exits.
If it does not obey to your effort to make it exit after days, then it is necessary that you bore around the arrow in the bone from every side with a fine lancet until the arrow enlarges. Then pull it out, and extract it.
If the arrow has already been hidden in a part of the body and is covered up and concealed from feeling: then look for it with probing (exploration). If you feel the arrow, then pull it out with some tools suitable for its extraction.
If you cannot get on top of it (arrow) due to the narrowness of the wound and because the arrow is long and deep; and there is no bone, nerve or vein there: then mark it over and wait until the wound expands and becomes possible to extract the arrow.
If there are ears attached to it (the arrow), then release the flesh fixed in them from all sides with all your strength and means that you can; and if you cannot get at the flesh by lifting it due to a fracture of the ears or twisting them, then wait until they come out.
And when you administer the extraction of the arrow, wherever it is, then use your hand's twisting motion with forks to all sides, until it comes out; and use subtle instruments with utmost subtlety so that the arrow does not break, and its pulling and extraction become difficult for you.
If you cannot open it within an hour, then leave it for some days until what is around it putrefies. Then proceed with it: the garment will be alleviated. If however it causes a flow of blood contrary to you, then administer what was said in the cure chapter, and guard your strength from cutting a vein, or nerve, or tendon.
And administer ingenuity in every possible way for the evacuation of the Arrowhead: and let it be with ease, calmness, or slowness, firmness as I told you.
It is necessary that when pulling the Arrow, it becomes weak in the shape it was at the time of the Arrow's fall upon itself. Since it is more useful (for the patient). If however this is not possible for you, then administer what is possible according to the figures.
The arrow that emerges from the opposite part or has already advanced towards the exterior: or it makes a hump at the end of the Arrow through sensation from the upper part of the skin near, and you see it protruding: then cut above it, and make the cut according to the size so that forceps can grasp it.
Then draw it out: for it becomes easy to come out.
If however it is retained in the bone, then twist your hand according to the roundness, until the Arrow impresses on the bone and expands for itself: then draw it out.
And if not: leave it for some days; then proceed with it until it comes out.
If the wood of the Arrow is in it, then push it along with it.
If however the wood fell out and you wish to administer the impulse: then insert a concave instrument into it so that it enters the concavity of the Arrowhead; then push it along with it.
If however the arrow is concave, then push it with the instrument entering into it. The arrow is pulled out by that (instrument).
If the arrow is poisoned, then it is necessary that you scrape all the concave flesh where the poison is; if it is possible for you to do so. Then treat it with what suits for that (purpose).
If the arrow falls in the chest, or in the stomach, or in the bladder, or in the side; and it is near where you touch with a probe, and it is possible for you to cut over it: then cut, and be careful not to cut a vein or a nerve, and extract it.
Then suture the wound, if you see (it), and treat it until it heals.
And this is the form of forceps with which arrows are extracted.
The ends should resemble a bird's beak, which have been made like iron: when they grip the arrow, they do not release it.
And they are made in various shapes, large, small, and medium-sized, according to the size of the arrow, its smallness, and the width and constriction of the wound.
The shape of a concave probe.
And the shape of a deaf probe.
This is the shape of a blunt end like a radius to pull into a concave arrow and push it with it.
Chapter on Phlebotomy of Veins.
Veins for bloodletting, whose practice has become common in the body, are thirty.
From the head, there are sixteen: two pulsating veins that are behind the ears, called two Herbs; and two arteries that appear at both temples; and two veins that are at both lacrimals of the two eyes, which are called Watchers; and a prepared vein in the middle of the forehead; and a vein at the tip of the nose. And two great veins, which are in the neck. And two veins that are in the upper lip of the mouth. And two veins that are in the lower lip. And these are the veins called Algecherich. And two veins that are under the tongue.
The veins that are bloodletted in the arms, are five in each arm.
One of them is the Brachial, and it is from the side of the forest; and the common people call it the vein of the head. And Black. And Middle. It is composed of a branch of the Basilica and a branch of the Brachial; and the common people call it the vein of the body. And Basilica, and it is placed on the domestic side; and it is also called Titillicaris. And the common people call it the vein of the belly. And the Sinew of the arm, and it is placed on the elbow; and it is the one that is bloodletted, and it is the one that appears above the thumb in a visible way.
And Saluatella: it is the vein that is between the ankles; and it has two branches.
And in the leg and in the foot, there are three veins. One of them is a curvature under the nail on the side of the little finger. And the second one is Saphena (Tendon): and its place is at the heel, on the inside domestic side. And vein of Nessus: and its place is at the heel, on the outside wild side.
And in the other thigh there are three veins similar to these (ones mentioned before).
Section on venesection of two veins after Ears.
However, the venesection of two veins that are after the Ears (the auricles), is beneficial for old catarrhs, and hemigraine, and sores on the head, old ones.
And the quality of performing venesection is as I explained (before). And it is to be shaved the head of the sick: and rub his neck in the place of two veins with a rough cloth for good friction. Then the sick person shall tighten his neck with a bandage until the two veins appear. And their places are behind the ears, in two depressed places of the head. Search for them with your finger, and where you feel their pulse under your finger, mark that place with a hot iron (encaustum).
Then take a surgical knife called an Incisor (Incidens) for venesection. Then insert the knife under the vein into the skin until the venesector reaches the bone. Then pull your hand with the vein and skin upwards and cut the vein with a cutting motion of the knife. And let the length of the incision be the size of two fingers joined or about that much: and make as much blood come out as you wish.
Then tie it with threads (ligatures) and let it heal.
And now we have reached the end of the book's section on incision and cauterization of these (veins).
The phlebotomy of the two arteries that are beneficial in two times is for the treatment of ancient migraine and sodam praua, and optalmia assidua, and for the discharge of acute superfluities towards the eyes.
And the quality of phlebotomy them (the arteries) is as I have told you. The sick person should tighten his neck very much until two veins appear to the perception with clear evidence and their pulse is shown under your finger. Then signs (are made) with hot iron (encaustic).
Then the skin is incised above the vein with your index finger and a lancet inserted below, and the vein is incised towards the top. And cut it (the vein) as you did with two other veins; and make the blood flow according to your need.
Then loosen the constriction of the sick person and place your finger on one side (of the vein). Then put a cloth and cotton over it, and tighten it from above with a strong constriction; and let it be until it is healed.
And now the remembrance of them (the veins), their incision, and their extraction at the beginning of the book has passed away.
But the benefit of the phlebotomy of the forehead vein is after the phlebotomy of the Cephalic (vein), for ancient facial ailments: and for migraine, redness, and foul ulcers.
And the quality of its (the forehead vein's) phlebotomy is as I have told you. Tie up tightly the neck of the sick man with a linen cloth until the vein appears. Then take the tool named Fossorium (Forceps).
Place the pointed end at the top of the Fossorium on the vein and strike it with a hammer or other tool in this way; then let the blood flow according to the amount you wish. Then release the incision of the sick man and tie it up until it is healed.
And sometimes it is opened with a broad-bladed leech (surgeon). However, it is not necessary that the surgeon have a sharp-pointed blade, like other surgeons do; instead, he should have one with a broad tip. And the surgeon shall make the incision obliquely, since the bone is close by; and perhaps the surgeon may break it if he is too sharp.
The remedy for the two veins that are in the two Lacrimal Ducts of both eyes is for inflammation and redness of the eye; and it is used on the eye itself, and for diseases of the face.
And the quality of phlebotomy of these veins is that the sick man ties his neck with a linen cloth (bandage). Then make the incision of the vein; you should stand over its head.
And make the incision obliquely in a short length with a small knife, for there is no flesh at that location. If the surgeon has a sharp-pointed blade, he may break it.
