Then let the blood flow as much as is necessary for you. Place a cloth over them and tie them up at night and release them in the morning.
The phlebotomy of the veins of the nose is useful for treating acute fever, severe itching, and facial ailments such as migraines and redness that occur on the face, especially when they are chronic.
And the quality of this phlebotomy is such that it tightens a weak neck. Then hold the nose with your left hand and take a subtle and long phlebotomy needle, and place it in the middle of the nostril, between that which divides the nose according to its straightness. Since the vein is not visible to the senses there and because blood begins to flow immediately.
And it is necessary that you hold the phlebotomy tool with your fingertips and let blood flow as needed. Then tie it up at night. It recovers quickly.
The phlebotomy of the veins of the neck has two beneficial effects: it helps to relieve coughing and prevent leprosy, melancholic ailments, impetigo and ulcers, as well as corrosive conditions such as herpes simplex.
And the quality of this phlebotomy is such that it tightens a weak neck with a band, and the worker should stand over the head of the sick person, who should be seated on a chair.
Then the phlebotomy needle should be inserted into the vein to a length slightly greater than that of the phlebotomy. Then extract an appropriate amount of blood, or according to what you see as necessary for this purpose. Then do the same with another vein.
Then untie the band and tighten two veins slightly so that the sick person does not faint. Let it (the wound) alone until morning. The wound heals.
On the phlebotomy of veins (Albucasis). The phlebotomy of veins by Albucasis is beneficial for the following: to prevent alcohol in the mouth, corruption of teeth and bad ulcers on lips, bad ulcers, even those that are in the nose and around it.
And the quality of their phlebotomy is as follows: make the sick person sit before you and tighten his neck with a bandage. Then turn his lip and look at two veins; one is on the right of the lip, and the second is on the left of it; and the demonstration of both is by showing their blackness. That is true because around them are small, dark veins. Therefore, make an incision on both of them with a cutting instrument.
If there is doubt concerning you and you do not know which one (vein) it is, then turn to the larger vein (incision), and its manifestation.
And similarly, do the same with the two veins that are on the upper lip; and it is most commonly used (phlebotomy) with this one; and make an incision in the two veins that are on the lower lip.
On phlebotomy of the vein under the tongue, after the phlebotomy of the jugular (cephalic) vein. But the phlebotomy of the two veins that are under the tongue is beneficial for: because the phlebotomy of the jugular (cephalic) vein is for afflictions that are in the throat, and diseases of the voice, and diseases of the mouth.
And the quality of their phlebotomy is as follows: make the sick person sit between your hands facing the sun; and lift his tongue, and look under the tongue at one vein on one side, and one vein on the other side; and observe that the color of both declines towards blackness.
Phlebotomy therefore both (of them): and guard, so that you do not injure your hand while performing phlebotomy. For there are arteries under it, and perhaps blood will flow from these arteries.
The three veins which are to be cut open at the elbow are those on which the practice of bloodletting is customary among all men.
And bloodletting of these is done in two ways. Either by pricking with a broad-bladed leech, or by oiling to the point of dripping, or by cutting with a sharp knife, and this is known as Alnaschar. And this is its form. (Note: The following passage describes the formation of the three veins
And from these are broad and narrow forms according to the size and tightness of the veins. The form of a broad-bladed leech is broad, as you see. And it is suitable for opening concave, full, protruding, thick veins that contain thick, turbid blood.
And this leech is narrower and more delicate at its extremity.
And it is suitable for bloodletting of the subtle veins that contain thin, yellowish blood.
The bloodletting of one of these three veins is beneficial for the basilica (large central hall or courtyard in a Roman bath complex). Because it draws blood from illnesses that are below the throat and neck, as they follow the chest and stomach, it is beneficial for the bloodletter to cut this vein, but he must be careful because there is an artery underneath it. If therefore he mistakes (it) and adds in the puncture of phlebotomy, he will cut the artery and there will be a flow of blood. Therefore it is necessary that (he) not phlebotomize her with the phlebotomist; rather he should be cutting with Alnesil.
If the Basilica does not appear by a manifest appearance, then it is necessary that (you) remove her and change (to) another, or seek some branch of it; or use a rope of its arm in place of the phlebotomist. For it (the Basilica) is manifest and cut her with phlebotomist Anesil, as we have said.
If however you wish to perform a phlebotomy on this one, then it is necessary before the constriction of the arm, that (you) touch the place, until (you) recognize the place of the pulse. Then mark a sign on the place with encaustum (pitch), afterwards the arm and cut the vein with a concave section of the phlebotomist Alnesil, as we have said. And beware that the blow does not fall into the space from the place of the artery.
And when you see that as you tighten the ligament, swelling at the place which you have marked with encaustum, then that swelling is the swelling of the artery; avoid it (the vein) therefore.
If you see blood rise up at the phlebotomy, like the urine of a child and it is thin, red: then know that it is from the blood of the artery. Then therefore hurry up and place your finger on it in a long space, afterwards remove your finger. If blood is cut off (many times it is cut off), then tighten the arm and release it; and let the weak avoid movement of it; let it be in fear of it.
And it should not move itself for some days, until it heals.
If the blood does not flow out and it chokes you; and there is no medicine present for you at the moment, then cut an artery, if it appears to you. For its ends contract, and blood flows out.
Either take the bark of a fig tree and wrap it; take one half, tie it over the place of the narrowed vein with a ligature and plasters until another day.
Then if blood flows out, it is good. And if not: then take care of it with the one who preceded it, remembering the place from which powder is removed for blood flow; and the removal of blood from it is not difficult in many dispositions, due to the small size of the wound, and the possibility of a ligature from the arm.
However, the benefit of venesection in the blue vein is that it draws blood from the upper part of the head and lower body; because it is composed of a branch of the Basilica vein, and the branch of the Cephalic vein, as we have said.
And it is suitable for the venesectionist that it be above fear of venesection from it, because under it is a nerve. If therefore the venesectionist adds in the puncture and finds a nerve, a stupor occurs, and its healing becomes difficult, and it may not heal completely.
And this nerve often appears to the sense. If it is hidden in some people and is subtle and not shown; then you should put your venesection where you can perform venesection with the Alnesil knife and avoid the nerve with your strength.
If a vein is between two nerves, then tie off the vein in length.
On the Vein of the Head. The vein of the head (Phlebotomia) is beneficial because it draws blood from the head and causes diseases of the eyes.
And it is proper in this vein (Phlebotomia) that if you wish, the phlebotomist should prick it with a lancet made of oil or myrtle, because it is the most pure of all veins, since it is neither an artery nor a nerve.
However, it is fitting for you at the phlebotomy (process of drawing blood), to show your head and arm only to the phlebotomist, and ask him to find a less painful place: and it will not harm him if he does not find the vein with the first blow, but he should try again on the same spot several times.
However, it may appear as a boil in some people when they are not bled with the first blow, but it does no harm to him.
On the utility of phlebotomy (bloodletting) and the precautions to be observed. The quality of the phlebotomy (bloodletting), and its accompanying circumstances, of which you should think about how to correct it.
First of all, you should know that phlebotomy is not administered unless for the conservation of health, persistence of it, and precautions against the onset of diseases.
And that a phlebotomy should be performed in one of the three veins which are in the elbow, namely the Head (Cephalica), Dark (Fusca), and Basilic. And that a phlebotomy should be performed at the beginning of spring during fasting, in spring and summer; and in autumn and winter after eating, when signs of fullness appear.
And let phlebotomy be performed on the first day or third day, after three hours have passed.
Phlebotomy administered in illnesses does not have a determined hour: but when necessity and suitability require it, it may be done at night or day, and in every hour, and at any time.
And it is not necessary to perform phlebotomy on boys until they reach fourteen years of age. Phlebotomy should not be performed on a pregnant woman except from the fourth to the seventh month. Phlebotomy should not be performed on the elderly who have already passed sixty years.
When anyone wishes to undergo phlebotomy for any reason, it is necessary that he cleanses his intestines before phlebotomy with a lenient enema if there is much retained feces in them: so that no putrid superfluities from the intestines attract veins during phlebotomy and harm the main organs.
Phlebotomy should not be performed on a patient who is vomiting, that is, suffering from nausea or satiety; nor on one who has drunk excessively and is still intoxicated, until that condition has been removed from them. Phlebotomy should not be performed on a sick person with dropsy.
Phlebotomy should be avoided again after a colic attack, vomiting, diarrhea, multiplication of sexual activity, labor, exercise, sleeplessness, fasting, and any other factor that weakens the strength of the body or an animal.
Then consider with subtlety the humors beforehand if the blood is thick due to food, drink, and medicine: if it is possible for him to do so.
Then enter a bath, if nothing prevents him from doing so; or exercise in some way to thin the blood.
Place phlebotomy on the chest, on the third day, as we said; and try to let it evacuate his chest that day from all the bad conditions of animals: such as tightness, anger, fear; and from all bodily conditions, such as labor, punishment, superfluity, and similar ones.
Let him place before himself the necessary items that human custom has run with, from which the practice of aromatics, fragrances, joy and similar things are present by the amount possible.
Then let the phlebotomist sit on the pulvinar, which shall be higher than the pulvinar for phlebotomy; afterwards he should pull out his arm, and the phlebotomist should rub it with his hand twice or thrice. Then tie the ligature with the cord and wind it around twice.
And let the incision be moderate, since an uneven incision, or excess in the incision, then prevents the flow of blood; or in healing, even then it also prevents the flow of blood.
Then after the incision, it is necessary that the person being bled rub his hands together until the veins swell and are felt.
Then the phlebotomist shall wash the place of bleeding with a little oil, his own.
Then he shall place his index finger of the left hand on the same vein at that spot where he wishes to make the incision, slightly; so that the vein does not run away, and the blow is not wasted. Because from the veins you find one running away like a cord during bleeding; and there is one that is full of windiness; and when you place an incision on it, it contracts under the person who is bleeding, and the person bleeding is deceived, and the cut does not open the vein; and if it opens it, it is closed; for this reason, therefore, the person bleeding must be firm and careful about all these matters.
Then let the blood-letter (phlebotomist) withdraw. If it is opened from one vein, then it is good. And if not, then do it again in another vein at the same place or above it with haste; but do not proceed if the spot is not punctured.
If the wound heals or the patient frightens, then let him go home for a day or two; and do not tighten the bandage. For perhaps it will draw an abscess (attrahit = it draws) which is hot. Do not enter the bath.
Then perform phlebotomy again if necessary.
If the opening is small or the blood runs subtly; fear that the quantity of blood that you wish will not exit; then perform phlebotomy again in the same hole correctly, and add slightly more in the procedure.
Do it quickly before the wound heals, for a healed wound is dangerous in many people during minor procedures.
If you see that the abscess has already healed, then do not perform any procedure on it at all; for it will not release anything from you. Place something made of oil cloth over it; because it softens the flow of blood. It is better in this place than olive oil itself and other types of oil.
And similarly administer the oil cloth in every phlebotomy of yours where you perform phlebotomy against the flow of blood; and this is done by Tyriacans, Alfarachs, and Sagzenes when placed on a spot.
For the blood becomes thin and dissolves when it is thick.
If a large abscess arises at the site of phlebotomy (accidat = happens), which often occurs, especially to those who are not skilled phlebotomists except for that vein or have a small opening of a vein; then hurry and place on the spot a sponge soaked in water and salt, heated slightly; and tighten it for one hour; because it dissolves.
And it is necessary that you do that after the outflow of blood from the vein itself, when its completion occurs, or from another vein.
If something remains in the place after several days that is dark or greenish, it does not harm it; and if you wish, put on top of it some aloe or myrrh dissolved; or something from the juice of mint, that is, camphor or similar to that.
And indeed often boils and eruptions occur near the venuepuncturing in the Basilica.
Then place your hand on it; then if you find softness under your pressure on it, then that eruption is bad. Be careful, therefore, not to put anything on it from what I said. For perhaps blood from an artery will flow out of it.
But make a plaster for him with the substance where there is stiffness, so that the place hardens. Then take care of him with the rest of the treatment until he is healed.
And it is necessary to extract blood from every man according to the quantity of its virtue. And what appears as a color spreading over the blood.
For if the blood is black, then let it flow out until it becomes red; and similarly, if you see that it is thick, then let it go until it becomes thinner; and similarly, if it is sharp, let it go until its sharpness is removed.
And it is necessary for him who is full, strong, and needs bloodletting once to have an increased bloodletting from the vein, and let him be a skilled bloodletter; but in the case of the weak one, let it be otherwise.
And it is necessary to extract it from the veins and that the opening be narrow; and it is better what is administered in bloodletting from the vein that it is a cross section, not a puncture.
This method of bloodletting is beneficial in preventing blood flow and nerve cutting; it is more praiseworthy and safer than venesection along both width and length.
Whoever is accustomed to faint during bloodletting should eat a little bread soaked in water with ground almonds before the bloodletting; or sekanjabin, if he is heated up: and extract from his blood three or four times.
If the patient's temperament is cold, then before bloodletting, he should receive bread soaked in honey-based syrup, or in a syrup flavored with honey and spices, or in scented aromatic syrup.
If fainting occurs during bloodletting and the cause is excessive blood flow, then it is necessary that he drinks water with meat juice and subtle scented syrup; uses aromatic substances with algalia and has his chest wrapped with it; and uses the remaining as we have said in the division of fainting due to evacuation (in chapter on fainting).
Whoever wants to let down his arm and release his blood through the second method should do so for a patient whose bloodletting is due to excessive evacuation and whose strength is weak. He should let out his blood gradually according to his strength for continuous days.
But whoever wants to let down his arm and release his blood through the second method and his body is full: then he should do that after seven or nine hours from his first bloodletting.
But whoever wants to draw blood from his body against the affected part: it should be done on the second or third day.
In a patient whose blood is already heated and sharpening and causing fever: it is necessary that his blood comes out once, and a large amount is taken out; and the incision should be enlarged until fainting occurs; after you have ensured all conditions of bloodletting; and you place your hand on his pulse near the course of blood, so that death does not occur due to fainting. "It often happens when the phlebotomator is ignorant, and falls due to negligence.

If you are during the days of summer, then you may excuse yourself from the repetition of hands in hot water. Much is put upon the course of blood in hot water during wintertime.

The aid of saphenous vein phlebotomy is for diseases located in the lower part of the body, such as uterine diseases, retention of menstruation, kidney weaknesses, old ulcers of hips and legs, and similar diseases.
The quality of phlebotomy is such that the sick person should insert his foot into warm water, and perform friction upon it until the veins swell; then tie above the joint of the foot with ligature. The vein location is at the calcaneum [heel bone], appearing towards the thumb; and they branch out from there into many branches on the face of the foot.
Therefore, phlebotomy should be done in a larger branch [of the vein] or at its confluence with the heel, because it is better and safer.
If phlebotomy is done on the face of the foot, then beware of the nerves that are beneath it on the surface of the foot.
And place your phlebotomy instrument at an angle, as if you wish to cut it; and the phlebotomist is Nesil.
If the exit of blood is forbidden, then immerse his foot in warm water; and let the blood flow into it until it has evacuated.
If the phlebotomist has missed a vein in the first attempt, then return to the same place with the phlebotomy but a little higher up, because the location is safe and there is no fear of harm from it, as long as you beware of the nerves, just as we have said.
And similarly with the saphenous vein of the other foot.
The location of the sciatic vein, as we have said, is at the heel on the side of the forest [the medial side].

On Ventosation, and the quality of its use.
They are made from horns, copper, and glass.
Ventosation is done in two ways. With scarification and bloodletting; the other is ventosation without scarification.
And this type of ventosation which is without scarification, is done in two ways. Or it is with fire. Or it is without fire.
And the locations of ventosation which are administered with scarification and bloodletting are fourteen on the body. One is the Ventosation of Alnocratis, and it is at the back of the head; Alkehel, and it is in the middle of the neck; ventosations of Alhagdahim, and there are two plates on the neck from both sides simultaneously; ventosations of the brain, and it is below the jaw on the lower part of the mouth; ventosations of the two spatulas; ventosations of Alachosos above the end of the tail; ventosations of the two pectoral glands, and they are in the middle of both arms; ventosations of the two legs; ventosations of the two kidneys.
And Ventosatio does not draw blood from you from the fine veins scattered in the flesh, and therefore its power does not fall out of them, as is the case with that incident from phlebotomy. It is not necessary to administer Ventosatio with fire, or without fire for any illness caused by congestion, until the entire body has been evacuated.
If necessity requires Ventosation due to illness or custom, we administer it at the beginning, middle, and end of every month, and at any other time when necessary.
Because there is a man who, when his blood is multiplied in him and he does not require its extraction through Ventosation, finds pain and swelling in his head.
And among them is one who finds congestion and redness on his face, his head, and his neck. And among them is one who finds itching on his face and forehead, and darkness and dullness in his eyes.
And there is one among them who scratches the place of his Ventosation. And among them is one whose laughter increases. And there is one among them who finds a taste of blood in his mouth: or the tongue is loosened and the blood is spat out. And among them is one whose sleep increases. And there is one among them who sees blood and redness in his sleep, as well as the dead, wounds, and things similar to those.
When we see something among them, especially if it is during the third quarter of the month: we then prescribe Ventosation to be performed after two or three hours have passed.
The benefit of Ventosation for the neck is that it alleviates heaviness in the head, and what is expelled is directed towards the eyes. It is necessary for it to be after evacuation of the entire body.
This [procedure] indeed is a ventose in place of cephalic phlebotomy.
It is not allowed for one with cold constitution to use it or in someone who has catarrh. For it does great harm to him [or her].
It is not proper for the elderly or someone with cold-related headaches to use it.
He who makes frequent use of it generates forgetfulness in him [or her].
Therefore, it is necessary for you to instruct the ventosator [operator] to withdraw his hand slightly downwards during venting to avoid generating forgetfulness.
Ventosation or Alkehel is in place of phlebotomy Fusca and basilica, and it causes coughing, constricted breathing, shortness of breath due to instrument splitting, coughing, stuffiness, and filling [of the body with air].
And it should be pushed slightly upwards during ventosation of Alkehel because if placed lower down, it generates weakness in the heart and stomach.
Ventosation alhagdahim causes headaches, migraines, eye pain, and tooth pain: it is in place of basilica phlebotomy.
You must instruct the ventosator not to press his hand during scarification to avoid cutting an artery and causing bleeding.
Ventosation under the chin causes alcohol in the mouth, corruption of gums, and similar symptoms such as those that occur in the mouth: it is in place of other forms of phlebotomy which are located on the two lips.
Ventosation with a spatula causes alchafacha, which comes from tension and heat.
Interior parts' ventosation provides help for phlebotomy of the three veins, Basilica, Fusca, and Cephalica: because it draws blood from all those small veins that are in flesh; and these small veins draw blood from larger ones until attraction reaches the three veins.
It is necessary that you warn the Ventosator not to proceed too much in scarification: because the place is bare of flesh, and beneath it are nerves and arteries.
The ventosa which is used for ventosation on anus, provides help for hemorrhoids of anus, and for lower ulcers.
It is necessary that you warn the Ventosator to have the ventosa large, and of air: because the place needs strong suction, and perhaps small ventosas break; and it should be scarified with a large scarifier.
The ventosae of legs diminish in a manifestly clear manner: because they draw blood from the whole body, and they provide old pains in kidneys, uterus, and bladder; they provoke menstruation, and they provide bothor, and ademenul; and they stand at the place of phlebotomy for two saphenas, and alhorcobeni.
However, they weaken the body greatly, and cause fainting to occur in many men.
And the help of ventosa alhorcobeni is close to the help of ventosa for two legs.
The quality of the position of ventosae is that you place the ventosa first in an empty area, and suck with moderate suction; and do not prolong the position of ventosae, but put them quickly, and remove them quickly, so that humors come to the place of arrival equally: and do not cease to repeat it and continue until you see the place already redden and swell, and the redness appears to be blood; then scarify, and gradually continue suction. Then consider in the arrangement of bodies.
For one who is from soft flesh and rare pores, it is necessary to make a single incision, not more: so that the place does not ulcerate.
And order the Wind-finder to enlarge the incision and not too deeply: and regulate the suction in ease, speed, and gentle motion.
If there is a thickening in the blood, then it is necessary to make an incision twice. In the first vein (artery), an opening is made for the subtle blood and its moisture; and in the second scrutiny for the extraction of thick blood.
If indeed the blood is very foul, then make an incision a third time: that it may follow the final one.
And at last, when we want to extract blood gradually or slightly, a single incision is sufficient for us. If we wish to extract much blood, make the incisions with multiple incisions.
And if we measure that the blood is thick, then it is necessary to make a deep incision: and the end equal in the incision is the depth of the skin.
What is that which must be administered with oils for the position of the Wind-finder, and what must the Wind-finder avoid?
In the one whose skin is thick, hard, dark, and the pores of which are narrow, it is necessary to anoint the places of the Wind-finder with oils that are opening, softening, resolving: if it is in summer, such as aloe oil, or olive oil, or oil from sweet almonds, or oil from squash seeds.
And if it is in winter, then use narcissus oil, or oil of the lily, or chamomile oil, or saffron: and similar to these.
If the swelling is large and cold, then use bitter almond oil, or balsam oil, or sweet almond oil, or sesame oil and similar ones.
If the swelling is small and soft, then oil should be prohibited. It is necessary that they rinse the affected areas with rose water after swelling has occurred, or with cold water, or with sea water, or with pumpkin juice, or with purslane water, and similar ones.
He whose blood is of great moisture, let him rinse the swellings with vinegar, or with water of myrtle, and sumac; and similar ones.
For whom the swellings are large, let him rinse his swellings with old wine, or with water of fennel, or with anise decoction, or chamomile; and similar ones.
It is necessary to avoid swelling in the bath or near the bath, rather it should be administered after leaving the bath for an hour, or for two hours.
It is not necessary that anyone sleeps after swelling.

And after that, it is necessary to consider first: then if the swelling or bleeding person is choleric and his acuity and inflammation overpowers his blood, then it is necessary for him to receive cooling agents, such as mulberry; also endive with vinegar and lettuce, and cucumber, and yarrow; and similar ones.
And let his food be chickens, and cooked sheep's meat, and condiments with sourness; and similar ones.
And for whom the complexion is cold, it is necessary to take syrup in drink made from honey, or almond, or yarrow seed; and drink medium-aged wine, which is between old and new; and it is ordered that he use sparingly food. Place his food with the young birds, and let him eat them: and swallow them, that is to say, small birds: and let him eat the young pigeons.
It is necessary that the drink on the day of venesection and phlebotomy be more than the food: and it is necessary that in some people in the hours of tyriaca alfaroch or medicine from musk, that is, muscat oil, or aleselithe be given before venesection: and before phlebotomy, or after: to strengthen the principal members, and to make the blood thin. And it is not necessary that they drink it heated.
Chapter on Venesection without Scarification. The venesections that are without scarification, are the venesections that are placed on the liver, and spleen, and podagra, and on the abdomen, navel, and places of kidneys, and bladder: because these members do not bear scarification upon them.
And it is not sought through them, except by the attraction of blood from one member to another: as we place the venesection on the breasts in the illness of nasal bleeding. And we use them to remove the cold venosity unappealing in one member from another: as we place the venesection on the abdomen and navel. For it dries the member, and warms it up, and removes pain because it has removed that venosity.
It is also placed on the kidneys when there is an obstruction, or a stone. By its virtue of attraction perhaps it opens up obstruction, or eradicates the stone from its place.
And similarly it does when placed on the liver and spleen for the venosity to be removed in both.
And the Ventosae are administered empty when cut only. And at times they are administered with fire.
And at times they are filled with warm water for pleurisy conditions; and this is to fill the Ventosa, and it should be large, with only hot water, or water in which cooked herbs that agree have been steeped.
Form of the Ventosa administered with fire.
The width of its opening should be two fingers open, as we have made; and the depth should be half the size of a palm; and on its side in the place that is approximately midway through it, there shall be a small hole, according to the size that can enter it with an awl; it should be made of bronze or citrine, smooth, equal edges, polished; so as not to harm the limb at its position above it; and in the middle of it there shall be a cross bar transverse from bronze or iron, where the candle is placed with fire.
And now this Ventosa is made large, larger than what we have described, but not much smaller, according to the conditions and age of the one who uses it.
For indeed, the Ventosa for children and weak ones are different from the Ventosa for men and robust bodies.
The quality of placing this Ventosa with fire on a limb is to burn linen cloth or a small candle from wax and place it on the middle cross bar that is in the center of the Ventosa; so that the fire is below the lower part of the Ventosa, so as not to burn the weak body.
Then it is placed on a limb; and the finger should be on the hole that I said, until we hold the Ventosa; when we need to, remove the finger, and the vapor will exit through the hole, and the Ventosa will break immediately.
Then heat the linen cloth according to its kind, and repeat if necessary.
In the plaster that is administered in case of pleurisy with water, there is no transverse rod nor hole: and it is not applied except because it is filled with water and placed only on the member.
And this is its form.
And this plaster is better when it is larger so that it can hold more water.
Chapter on Suspension of Sanguisugarum (Sugarcane).
Sugarcane juice is not administered in many places except where it is impossible to position fumigating vapors due to their smallness, such as lips, gums, and similar places. Or on a naked member like the finger or nose, and similar places.
And the quality of using them is: to come from sugarcane that are in sweet, clean waters free from decay.
Then they are left day and night in sweet water until they become hungry and there remains nothing in their stomachs. Afterwards, the body should be evacuated first with bloodletting and with fumigating vapors. Then clean the sick member until it turns red. Afterwards, they are placed upon it.
When they are filled and fall, and it is possible to suck the place with fumigating vapors, then it is more effective in helping: and if not, then clean the place with vinegar. Afterwards, when much water is added, it is rubbed and pressed out.
If the course of blood continues after the use of sugarcane, and it needs to be sweated out, then immerse a linen cloth in cold water and place it on top until the perspiration stops.
If blood is multiplied, then sprinkle on it powdered zegi that has been crushed, or gall, and similar things from spices: until the blood is cut off.
Or place pieces of peeled beans there: and allow them to remain until the beans stick to the place, for the blood is cut off when this happens.
And it is necessary that, if the use of Sanguisugum is required, they should be changed when possible.
But if Sanguisugae are forbidden from suspension, then the place with fresh blood is washed. Or insert a lancet into the places until something comes out of the blood. Then they should be placed, for they themselves will immediately stick when they sense something from the blood.
When it is desired that they fall, sprinkle something from crushed aloes onto them, or salt, or ashes: and they will immediately fall.

Chapter II.
It is necessary that we state in the preface of this Chapter, the main points and headings, to which the understanding of the craftsman is first required regarding their truth; namely that you, and those who are quick, should know or learn this noble art, and others from you.
Therefore, I say that when any fracture, dislocation, twisting, or accident happens, then it is necessary to rush at once to bleed him and solve the problem, or both at the same time; unless something forbids it, such as weakness of strength; or he who has it is a child, an old man weakened, or there is a time of intense heat, or very intense cold.
Then let him be content with his food, cold vegetables, and turnips, and beans; and let wine, heavy meats, and filling foods from every kind of food that fills the veins with blood be prohibited. Until you are secure from hot fever and not fearful of the emission of matter to the limb; then let the sick person return first to his own diet with which his custom is accustomed.
When the broken bone begins to heal, it is then necessary that the sick person be fed with nourishing foods in abundance, thick, heavy, containing viscosity; such as food made from cooked wheat with water, rice, and heads, and feet, and entrails of cattle, and eggs, and fresh fish; and thick wine, and similar things. When this regimen is applied, the healing of the fracture occurs more quickly and better.
And know that broken bones, in men who are in the first old age and are weakened, cannot always be united and consolidated according to their nature, due to drying out of their members; and they consolidate and unite what is from them at the end of softness, such as the bones of small children.
Nevertheless, nature annexes something resembling glue to the broken bone from all sides, in which there is thickness, with which it adheres and tightens it until they are consolidated against each other and bound together until it comes to the end of strength and firmness, as it was at first, until it does not prohibit anything from its operations; and for this reason it is necessary that you place nourishment for the sick with heavy foods, containing viscosity and thickness, as we have said.
And know that the types of fractures are diverse, according to the diversity of the members; because a fracture of the leg is different from a fracture of the bone of the head: and a fracture of the bone of the chest is different from a fracture of the bone of the back: and similarly all other members are different from each other; and I come with a reminder of every type of fracture exposed in its own chapter. The species of bone fracture are diversified even within themselves.
Since the fracture of it is curved, what happen in it are also stars (or studs). And sometimes the fracture of it happens along the length of the bone; and there are fractures called stars (or studs), that is, studs, and additions coming forth, as in a reed when a knife is prepared for writing. And not proceeding; and it is a fracture with wound and rupture at the head; and it is a small cleft fracture.
And each species has its skill, or proper tool for its restoration, according to which its memory will come, distinguished in their places.
And from that which the bone fracture is known, there is its curvature, and its prominence; and its appearance to the sense, and its sound when you press it with your hand.
When therefore the curvature is not appearing in place, nor a sound when you touch it with pressure on the bone, nor does it find a large pain; then there is no fracture there, indeed it is possible that it is a curvature or a small cleft fracture; therefore, do not move it with your hand and compression at all, but rather put some medicine over it where its memory is, after the appropriate time has passed; then compress the bandage with subtle tension.
And know that when the place is curved and split into two, except for studs (or stars), remove all parts from their natural position; then it will be necessary for you to begin at your hour for its correction, equalizing it before a hot abscess occurs.
If, however, a hot abscess occurs, then let it be for several days until the abscess subsides. Then, cut it evenly according to whatever method can be done with kindness and skill.
And know that the restoration of it, and its alignment, is easier than bone, in which complications may occur.
Then tie it, as your memory recalls it.
If there are complications in the bone, then it is necessary to extend this fractured limb from both parts, whether it be a hand or foot; or with your hand, if the member is small; or with two ropes, or with a rope and your hand.
Let the position in which you place the limb be on an equal plane, according to its natural shape, until the parts of the fractured member are extended; then try to bring back those additions with all your skill, and according to whatever method you can do it in their places: and take care not to cause pain or injury due to your operation; and try, according to your strength, to apply one bone to its companion, according to the best forms.
It is necessary in that hour for you to test and touch it with your hand. If therefore you see something different there, correct it, and align it by the quantity of your skill.
And beware of excessive stretching and strong compression, as many foolish people do. For many times they cause abscesses or contractions to occur in the member through their operation; as we have already seen it happen many times through their operations.
Then join after the alignment and proper operation, and binding and rest of this member, and peace and calmness; and warn the weak person about it. And make him be careful not to move it in the hour of his waking, and sleep, and turning, and restlessness, and all his movements, with all his strength. And so that it may be avoided, let there not be a position of a member with which follows pain. When he feels pain or injury while in the position of a member: then it is necessary to move him to another position from which he does not feel pain.
And so let that be avoided, that his position is uneven or twisted: lest there occur a twist in the member when he is to be restored.
The quality of the binding for a broken member is as I have described.
Know that broken members are differentiated by their size, and the extent of their breakage, and their shape.
Those which are small, such as the arm, and finger, and shin-bone, and similar to these, then it is necessary that there be a rolling of soft, fine cloth over them. But those which are large, such as the hip and thigh, it is necessary that there be a broad, hard rolling over them.
Because a wide band joins a large member and binds it all around with an equally tight binding so that no stain penetrates it.
Then after your evacuation, place an appropriate linen on it, in soft woolen over the place of the break itself. Then begin to wrap the bandage over the place of the break itself three or four times, according to what the member deserves; and tighten your hand slightly with the bandage.
Then go to the upper part away from the place of the break and bind it less than your previous binding that you applied to the broken place. Then withdraw with the bandage from the place of the break slightly and soften the binding gradually until you have taken a large amount from a healthy place.
Then take another dressing and wrap it again over the broken place with bandages. After moving with the incline towards the lower part from the fracture. And your operation shall be in the stricture of the incline and its softening, according to what we said in the first higher incline.
Then place between the inclines from soft wool or cloth that matches the twisting of the fracture, if there is twisting in it; and if not, do not place anything in it.
Then incline over the same wound another. Afterwards, place evenly on these inclines stars properly made immediately, if there is no swelling or hot abscess.
If there is swelling or abscess in it; then place over it what sits on that abscess, and remove the swelling; and let it remain for some days. Then bind over them the stars.
The stars shall be from the middle parts of wide reeds, shaved and skillfully formed; or they shall be made of wood from which cribra are made, which are made from pine, yew, palm branches or calingi; and let the width of all the stars be according to this form and this same shape.
However, it is necessary that the star placed over the tendon should be slightly larger and wider than the others; and the length of the star shall be according to what allows the limb in terms of size and smallness.
Then bind over the stars with another bandage, according to the first stricture. Then bind over it with threads properly, according to what we said about the stricture; and your stricture shall be above the fracture more; and the greater the distance from the fracture, the less the stricture.
It is necessary that the threads be of medium thickness and fineness, and be made of soft flax.
If threads are thick, as I saw in the operation of fools tying threads twisted from flax cords, it is a great error: because a constriction falls out with them beyond quality.
Fine threads do not agree again: because you will not achieve what you want through the constriction with them.
And it is not proper that there be less than a finger's breadth between the anvil and the hammer.
If the weak [person] is injured by the extremities of the anvils after the constriction in healthy places: then place under them soft wool or carded wool until nothing is injured from that.
But when there is a fracture with a wound and there occurs disruption in the skin, I will come with its discussion in chapter two under special cases.
And know that not every broken limb should be tied with anvils from the first day. What is meant by this, is that when a limb is large, you should not place superior anvils on it unless after five days, or seven, or more, according to your safety from the risk of cold abscesses.
This is the method of plasters which the ancients used to restore, which are placed on a fracture and dislocation.
Â¶ Common Plaster Method abbreviated, restoring a fracture, suitable for many constitutions, particularly women and children: because it is from that which heat does not conquer nor cold.
And it is to receive from the flour mill dust and it is the marrow of the wheat which hangs on the walls of the mill near the movement of the stones: and make this as it is, except that you sift it with egg white: and place its composition not thick, nor thin. Then use it.
Â¶ Another Plaster Method, restoring a fracture, dislocation and twisting. Prepare almess, laudanum, acacia, enula, and mugath. Grind all of them until they are as fine as ten parts of myrrh, five parts of aloes, and twenty parts of alethil. Sieve them and mix with alethil water, or with sheep's white grease if the disease is hot. Then use this plaster. For it is of good composition, restores broken bones quickly, and benefits many people.
Another method for the plaster which also treats fractures and dislocations. Prepare mugath, mess, white alteae, all of them pounded until as fine as ten parts of myrrh, five parts of aloes, six parts of acacia, twenty parts of Armenian pounded boli, and sieve them. Mix with water or sheep's white grease and administer.
Another method for the plaster for joining bones and separating them from their places: it relieves pain and swelling in those places, and heals broken bones and fractures. Take boiled wool, immerse it in vinegar and hot oil, then place it over the affected area.
In this plaster there is no healing power, but it is good for the relief of hot abscesses and it effectively drives out pains.
Another method for the plaster that restores a broken bone. Take the leaves of the fig tree and the wild papaver: grind them all and make a plaster from both.
Another short method for the plaster administered in the healing of a broken bone, and when you wish to resolve the remaining abscess. Prepare the root of alteae, chamomile, flowers of violets, and herb flour, all one part: grind them all and mix with wine if the limb is not heated.
If it is heated (the wound), then make that with water of coriander (juice) or with water and use it.
Another stronger method for healing, stronger than the first, when a hard abscess occurs during restoration of a limb. Take equal parts of althea root, linseed, balaustia, lentil, samsuc, viole flowers, and chamomile. Grind all together and make a paste with water of willow, sweet water, or wine. Apply the above to the member according to its heat and calmness of the heat.
Hippocrates himself did not say in his book that it should be placed on a fractured member during its healing, except for plaster made from tallow and oil, not anything else; and he said that it should have a middle state between thickness and subtlety.
Galenus, however, saw fit that they should be placed on a fractured member during its healing, things that have the dryness of heat, such as myrrh and aloes and olibanum and similar ones mentioned earlier.
Method of plaster for injury and pain relief. Take equal parts of mugath (althea), chickpeas, human hairs (or bird feathers), althea root, and salt. Grind and sieve and make a paste from it; and make plaster out of it.
The amount by which the ligature should remain, then dissolve, is such that you consider whether pain does not occur, nor itching, nor is the bone moved from its place; then do not dissolve it for many days. If itching or discomfort or swelling occurs in the place; then hurry and dissolve it within an hour and remove the plaster from it; and take a soft cloth or a soft sea sponge moistened; and submerge it in warm water and wash the place with it until the itching subsides and the pain is soothed.
If itching or discomfort or swelling occurs in the place; then hurry and dissolve it within an hour and remove the plaster from it; and take a soft cloth or a soft sea sponge moistened; and submerge it in warm water and wash the place with it until the itching subsides and the pain is soothed. Then let the member rest for one hour. Then place a scalded wool upon it, immerse it in vinegar and oil (or rose oil), or oil: and bind it on top of it at night until you are secure from the hot abscess, and let the swelling of the member subside, and remove its pain.
Then repeat it for a delicate bandage and a small plaster: and do not bind it tightly with your first constraint, and delicately with it until it heals.
If you see that the abscess, redness, pain, swelling and all other symptoms are already completely removed, and if you need plaster and bandage, then repeat it as you did at first equally.
If nothing of what we said happens in the member, then do not loosen it, unless after three days, or four, or five, or seven. And when it is released after twenty days, according to what appears to you from the loosening of the member, as we said, until when a fracture adheres and coagulation of the flesh approaches on it: add also in the bandage more than your first binding: and add also in the thickening of food for the sick, according to what was previously remembered.
If you see a place of the fracture already to dry out and thin more than is necessary: then know that nutrition is prevented from reaching it. Therefore, anoint the member with warm water at every corner where you loosen it, and let it be for all three days, and rub the bandage slightly.
By this operation, nourishment flows to the member, and it heals quickly.
But what the foolish restorers do from a fracture of the member instead of first restoring it as is necessary, and they restore it according to crookedness, this is an error in their operation, and there is great fear: and if it were right, they would have said that in their old books, and operated with it.
And I have nowhere seen their trace in that entirely, and it is more correct that it should not be done.
On the making of a fracture in the head.
Types of fractures are diverse, and their forms many; and their causes are distinguished.
For there is a fracture which occurs from striking with a sword; and the blow removes bone completely as far as it penetrates into the siphac, which is beneath the bone, just as an ax does in wood; and for this reason this type of fracture is called Axial.
Or the sword cuts something of a bone and cuts its face, but does not penetrate with the cut to its end; and this type of fracture is called Kalan, that is, Absolute Lever.
And there is a wound of two types of fractures, either large or small.
And from a fracture there is one that is a crushing or grinding; and its cause is a blow with a stone, or falling on a stone, or similar things.
And this fracture is also penetrating, which approaches the membrane, which is beneath the bone; or it is on the face of a bone; and this type of fracture has two forms, either small or large.
And from a fracture there is one that is hidden in the bone in its finest part; and it is a small crack, and for this reason this type of fracture is called Capillar.
And among them there is a fracture that occurs from falling or striking with a stone, and similar things; which make the layer of the bone enter within, and it forms a hollow in its place; just as happens to the caldrons of brass when they are struck, and part of them enters within.
And this indeed often occurs in the bones of moist heads, such as those of children's heads.
And all these types of fractures of the sternum are separate, and I shall come with the recollection of that entire thing in its place.
And we know all these types of fractures by detection over it and examination of them with tests, and removal of the corrupted flesh that is over them.
The Capillary type is known by this: it is detected over the bone, scraped off and then bound over it with pitch; for the fracture appears black.
In the treatment of a fracture, consider first the condition of the patient: then, if you see signs in his condition that indicate fear, such as cholera vomiting, extension, loss of intelligence, loss of voice, fainting, acute fever, eye discharge, redness of eyes, and similar signs in many other conditions, it is most likely not to heal; and if you see signs that do not frighten you and you hope for recovery: then begin the treatment.
If someone comes to you wounded first with the wound, and it is in winter days, then it is necessary that you strive for the removal of the bone before the fourteenth day according to all circumstances.
And if it is in summer days, then it is necessary that you hurry to remove the bone before the seventh day, before what is under the bone from fat corrupts and the signs that we have mentioned occur.
If the fracture of the bone has already reached the fat touching the brain, and is with grating and pulling: then it is necessary that you cut the broken and ground part, as I tell you.
And it is necessary that you shave the head of the wounded person: and scrape over the bone in any way possible for you: and according to the shape of the wound, and what is light for the patient.
If it happens to you during bone examination, a blood flow or a hot abscess: then oppose him with what is necessary. And it is to be filled with linen soaked in wine and rose oil, and let the wound rest until the abscess settles down, and be careful of bleeding. Then begin in the cure of the bone, and its removal.
And this is done according to one of two methods.
One method is to cut the bone with a curved blade: and this is its shape.
Then administer another scalpel, broader but shorter than the first: and this is its shape.
Then also administer another scalpel wider than the second.
And the second method is to have a number of scalpels of different sizes, some wider than others and some shorter; they should be at the end of their sharpness, and made of iron or bronze; use them with ease when striking the scalpel so as not to strike the head and injure it.
If the bone is hard: then it is necessary to bore around it before you administer the scalpel, with the chisels that are called terebras, which do not penetrate deeply; they are not called so because they do not pass beyond the limit of the bone to what is behind it; because the terebra has a rounded end above that which is underneath the head and is sharp like an edge, and a small circle prevents it from sinking and penetrating the thickness of the bone.
It is also suitable for you to take a large number of these chisels, one of which corresponds to the thickness of the bone, until there are as many terebras as the skull requires; the sharpness of each should be according to the thickness of that skull.
And this is the form of three species of trephines, or trepans.
The quality of perforating around a broken bone is as follows: you should place the trepan on the bone and turn it with your fingers until you know that it has already penetrated the bone. Then change the trepan to another place, and place there that which is between every hole of such size as a finger's breadth or approximately so.
Then cut with the saw what is between every hole in the bone, and make it finer than possible at the end of the thinness, as we have said, until you have chiseled the bone, either with your hand or with some other tools which you have prepared for that purpose, such as tenaliae and fine forpices.
And it is necessary that you take every care to ensure that the trepan or saw does not touch anything from the skin.
When you therefore perforate the bone by circumscribing it and it has been separated from the skin, if it is continuous with it, and you extract it, it is necessary that you grind and level off the extremity which remains in the bone with another similar saw. However, it should be thin and delicate compared to other saws.
If something remains of the small bones and splinters, then take them with ease, with that which is at hand from your tools. Then care for the wound afterwards with linen and ointments as we have said.
And another method of operation is easy, entirely far from fear; which Galen said and praised greatly; and this is his saying. It is necessary for you first to begin by exposing the bone from the place where the fracture is more intense and clearer; until when you have exposed that part and placed under it the extremity of this saw with a concave shape, which has this form.
The rounded part (of the eye) cuts nothing gently: and the sharp part of it proceeding in both its sides as you see, so that the rounded part of the lens is attached to the sclera, with its sharp edge incident on the bone.
Then strike upon the Incisor with a small hammer until you cut through the bone easily, as it is usually turned (in operation): and be careful that no cloth slips into what is being cut, which you fear might happen altogether.
If the physician were not more foolish than men, nor more ignorant of his craft, nor asleep, then if something remained attached to the bone from the cloth in certain places, he would cut it away with the sharp edge of the incisor's lenticular part and free it easily. It is freed from it without harm and without fear.
If the bone is not broken through to the cloth, and what is being cut away from the bone is its face, with roughness and subtle irregularities remaining in it: then it is necessary that you smooth out that roughness and remove those irregularities with fine rasps which you have prepared in various quantities, so that it is possible for you to administer more suitable and correct ones according to the procedure of the operation, and the broken bone and its shape. And it is necessary that you administer according to your judgment, first smoothing the bone more broadly with those rasps. Then use a finer one after that.
And it is necessary that you administer according to your judgment, first smoothing the bone more broadly with those rasps. Then use a finer one after that. And do not cease to do this in continuity until you come to administer more thinly and finer of them all.
In each of the remaining small cracks and a fine break, care must be taken according to what leads to its correction.
And it is a matter that cannot be hidden upon all, to which in this art there is any study, and it stood upon what we have written, and we showed in large fractures.
If however a piece of cloth is found to be retained when you excise bone: then it is necessary that you take linen cloth according to the size of the wound, and immerse it in rose oil, and place it on the opening of the wound. Then take another piece of cloth, either doubled or tripled, and immerse it in wine and rose oil, and place it on top of the first one; and do that as easily as possible above it, so that it is not heavy.
Then administer on top of it a wide bandage; and do not tighten it except according to the size that holds the wound only; and release it with this care for one day or two days until you are assured from hot inflammation. Then dissolve it (the bandage), and administer afterwards medications with which excessive dryness is superfluous; such as the roots of garlic, and flour of herbs, and powder of incense, and aristolochia, and similar ones: and it is that you make from these medications a powder, which is to be sprinkled on the wound like dry ones.
And universally administer on it all medicine with the property of purifying and not biting. And be careful according to your ability that the wound is clean during your treatment; neither let there be filth from ointments, nor dirtiness.
And be careful according to your ability that the wound is clean during your treatment; neither let there be filth from ointments, nor dirtiness. Neither let the pus gather in it entirely; because when pus gathers on a cerebral cloth, it corrupts it and rots itself, and great evil follows after that for the patient: and already it has happened in the head, when the bone is cleaned from it, especially when care is neglected for it, blackness appears on its surface.
Consider then, if afflictions occur to the sick as we have said, know that it is undoubtedly dangerous.
If there is no discoloration (nigredo) unless it arises from the medication placed upon it and it is in the virtue of that medicine to cause discoloration: then you should take a portion from honey, three portions from rose oil, and anoint with them a cloth. Then place it on a sheep's bladder (siphac): afterwards treat it with the remedies suitable for healing until it is healed.
[Section heading: Restoration of the nose when broken.]
Know that the nose is not broken except by the two upper parts of it together or one of them: because they consist of two bones; since its lower part (inferius) is cartilaginous, it does not break and does not happen unless there is rubbing, distortion, and deviation.
If one of the two parts is broken, then it is necessary that you insert a small finger into the nose's opening and hold the fracture with your index and thumb from the outside until the nose returns to its natural shape; this should be done skillfully and easily. And beware that your treatment does not cause pain to the sick person.
If the fracture is in the upper part of the nose and the finger does not reach it: then you should apply pressure to the end of the radius where there is a slight swelling.
If the fracture is in two parts, then make a similar one and approach with its healing on the first day of the fracture if possible. And if not, then after the seventh or tenth day in the presence of warm cauterizers (apostematis).
Then insert a strip of linen from a linen cloth into the end of the nose if the fracture is in one part; or insert two strips of linen if the fracture is in two parts: and let there be a swelling of sufficient size in the linen to fill the opening of the nose.
And certain restorers of old times said, that linen should be soaked in butter and changed every day. That rather does not seem to me, but it is necessary that linen be soaked in cow's milk, prepared with miller's flour. Then let the linen be removed when the bone hardens and the cartilage hardens.
And sometimes a goose feather is inserted into the place of linen in the nose, after a soft cloth has been laid upon it; and this is done so that the linen holds the fracture of the nose more strongly, and so that the weak are not hindered from breathing.
And this is not necessary. If you wish, do it; and if you wish, do linen.
And if it happens that the nose again when treating an abscess while operating, pack the nose with hot plasters, or with cerotic, or with pitch softened in rose oil, or a little bit of aloes.
But if an abscess does not occur, then it is necessary that the pack be made with sesame flour and incense powder prepared with cow's milk. Then place a soft wool on top of it, and do not tie the nose with anything at all.
If the bones of the nose are broken by a small fracture or crushed, then it is necessary that you cut above them and extract them with instruments suitable for them. Then be cauterized and care for it with that which adheres to it, and let it heal with suitable packs or ointments.
If there is a wound between the nose and lip, then it is necessary to care for it with linen; and administer a lead tube until it heals.
Chapter on the restoration of the lower jaw when it breaks.
When the lower jaw is disrupted and its fracture is not accompanied by a wound: then examine whether its fracture is only on the outside and it does not break into two, and it bends towards the inside: then its recognition is easy.
Therefore, if the fracture is in the right part of it (the jaw), insert your left index finger into the weak bone: similarly, if the fracture is in the left lower jaw, then insert your right index finger: and push with it the bulge of the fracture towards the outside with ease from within the bone using your other hand properly from the inside part of the bone, and with it equalize it.
If the fracture of the lower jaw is already separated into two, then it is necessary to administer extension from both parts, according to rectitude: until it is possible to equalize it.
If however, disturbance or disruption has already occurred among the teeth, then bind those which you hope will remain with a thread of gold, silver, silk, or floss. Then place upon the broken lower jaw pieces of lead. Then place over it a double cloth and put over the cloth a large star-shaped plaster well made: or an equal piece of shoe sole leather in length of the lower jaw.
Then bind it above (the dressing), according to the preparation of its binding and the fitting of its application, until it is not destroyed: and order the patient to rest quietly: and put his softened food.
If you think that something has changed its shape by some means: then hurry and undo it on the third day. Then straighten what has been changed from it, and plaster it with flour mill powder, with egg white or wheat flour, after you have removed the lead: and place over the plaster soft wool.
While that adhesive plaster remains on it and the bone arrangement does not change, leave it undisturbed until it heals; or tie up the fracture. This fracture is often tied for three weeks.
If a warm abscess occurs meanwhile, then administer what I said earlier for its relief until the abscess is removed.
And if there is a fracture with a wound, consider. If the bone fragment is already separating from the bone or the flesh, then be careful with this. Use fine instruments to remove the bone fragments that are suitable for you in removing them from the instruments.
If the wound opening is narrowed, then expand it with a leech according to your needs.
Then when you remove the flesh, and nothing remains of it, or if the wound opening is large: and if not, cover it with plasters that are suitable for this, and let it heal until it heals.
[Title] On the restoration of a fork when it breaks.
Much indeed is broken by the Furcula from the anterior part, near the humerus, and its fracture is made according to one of the three modes.
Either it is broken and separated into two, apart from which complications may occur within it; and it is easier for its restoration.
Or, so that complications occur in the bone itself; and it is difficult for its restoration.
Or, so that it is a fracture with wounding.
And the procedure for it when the fracture is without wounding is as follows: make two attendants present, so that one of them holds the assisting hand that follows the broken Furcula, and the other stretches the neck towards the side.
Then you must equalize the fracture with your fingers until it takes on its form, as is required; there should be no prominence nor hollow within it.
If you require more stretching, then it is necessary that you place a small ball made of cloth or wool beneath the weakened patient's neck, and its size should correspond to your need; and stretch, lift, and apply the ball with your hand until you equalize the fracture, as is required.
If you cannot draw out the extremity of the Furcula to the outside because it has reached deep within; then it is necessary that the weakened patient lies on his neck and places a splint of medium size under his shoulder; and press an assistant on his humerus towards the lower part, so that the bone of the Furcula in the deep part is lifted upwards; at that time, correct the fracture and equalize it with your fingers.
If you feel a splinter from the Furcula and it moves; then it is necessary that you probe over it and draw out the splinter easily.
If it is retained in the bone, then prepare a probe for its incision with one of the Incisorium you have prepared for that purpose; after placing an instrument under the Furcula to protect the Sifach (ligament), and this instrument can be made of wood or iron. And this is its shape.
It resembles a snail without a hollow part.
Let its width be according to that which you require for it depending on the size of the bone and its smallness. The length (of it) should also be, as much as possible, for your operation.
It shall have two ends, as you see: one wide, and the other narrow.
If however the wound split which you cut when you pulled out the splinter of bone, is wide, and you are free from heat: then gather together two lips of the wound with suture.
And if the split is small, or you fear a hot abscess: then fill the wound with cloths, and lint according to the size of the wound cut.
If a hot abscess occurs, then pour oil of rose into it, and vinegar, and wine, and place (something) on top of it.
The narrowing (of a bone) without wound and without cutting, is to place on the bone a plaster made from flour milled with egg white. And place a soft cushion over it. Then place a sphere under the sensitive part of it, if needed. Then take a roll (tourniquet), that which the Saracens hold, which is very long: and its width should be as large as one palm or approximately that. Then place lint (together) on top of the cushion and plaster. Then take a needle from a delicate wood, whose width is three fingers, and similarly in length. Then insert it into the cloth. Then insert the cloth with the needle at the place of the bleeding that falls upon the fracture and place. Then place the astringent (tourniquet) upon the fracture as it is turned, and insert it upon its healthy part and its weak part: and tie it over all parts, so that the narrowing holds the fracture properly: and this is what must not be concealed from you, and all of it, so that the needle does not move from the broken bone. It is necessary that you examine the sick person every day. Whenever the ligament becomes soft and you see the splinter being removed, then adjust it and tighten the ligament.
Then place the sick person's sleep on his back and put a small pebble under his pulse during his sleep so that when he is awakened, help will be near him from the side of himself. The break in the collarbone is to be treated by elevating the shoulder.
Or bind the arm to the neck; and do not loosen the binding, unless it happens in the place of occurrence from itching or from an abscess, for twelve days.
Then renew the plaster if you see his face turning towards it. And add a small amount to the constriction, and let it go until it heals and hardens.
The break in the collarbone is tightened and supported most for twenty days; and sometimes it happens in fewer than that number of days in some people.
Chapter on the healing of a broken oar.
Rarely does an oar break at its wide part; and it is not broken except from the edges of it.
When it breaks at its wide part and breaks in the middle, and this is not known except by touch; then prepare for it according to the shape of the break. Reduce it to its natural form by any means that is possible for you.
Then put on the place flour of milling with egg white and soft wool, and put a small cloth on top. Then place over it a splint made of thin wood according to the size of the oar, or slightly larger but not much so.
If there is a hollow under the star (mark) on any spatula's handle, then fill that hollow with soft wax until the mark sinks down evenly.
Then coat it on top with long alum paste, with appropriate constriction and strength of the star, until it is not removed from its place.
And consider the ligature (bandage) each day. Whenever the ligature softens, coat it and if the star moves from its place, let it lie weak on its healthy side.
The spatula is restored within twenty or thirty days.
When this number (20 or 30) is complete, then loosen the ligature: and you will be safe. For it (the spatula handle) is made of bones whose fracture is not feared, nor their solution.
If a splinter comes out of the bone and pierces under the skin, then cut above it and remove it. And do as we have said in the treatment of a dislocated collarbone with warm poultice, if something happens from it (the spatula handle).
Chapter on the restoration of a broken breastbone.
The breastbone sometimes breaks in half and this rarely happens. Its extremities break more often and are crushed due to accidents of it.
When its middle breaks and declines towards the lower part, and it causes a violent pain, difficulty in breathing, coughing, and possibly vomiting blood, and a hollow is declared on the broken bone and not concealed from the sense, the restoration of it is that it lies weak on its back: and place between the spatulas its pulvinar.
Then press the arms and bring the ribs from both sides or flanks with hands: and grind it evenly in accordance with any way that can be possible for it to return to the shape of the bone, as is necessary.
Then place upon it an ointment and a stopper, and on top of it an astel from subtle wood (such as willow or alder), or chalking (a type of plaster) and similar ones, after you have rolled it up in a cloth. Then bind it firmly to the broken bone so that it is not moved: and go with the bandage according to its round shape towards the back several times, and tighten it appropriately using a suitable ligature.
Then consider the bandage in every hour, and whenever it becomes soft, tighten it; and if the need for its solution arises due to itching, corrosion that occurs at the place, or pain, or abscess: then go, unbind it, remove the ointment, and correct whatever has happened with the treatment method.
Then reduce the ointment if you see it facing that way; and connect a ligature until it heals.
Chapter on the restoration of ribs, when they are broken.
Know that a rib fracture does not occur except in thick places following the back, and from the extremities of them from the anterior part. And it does not occur by rubbing, unless because they are cartilaginous. And the knowledge of this is not hidden from sensation during examination with fingers.
And the restoration of it is done so that the fracture is aligned with fingers to the extent possible until it is equal in shape as required. Then apply ointment and bind the broken bone with a stopper if needed.
If the rib fracture is sloping towards the inside: then a severe pain occurs, and puncture, just like the puncture that happens to one who has pleurisy: because the bone pierces the membrane (pleura), and also difficulty in breathing, coughing, and vomiting blood frequently; and this is difficult to treat.
And the ancients were skilled in that [surgery] with many devices: and among them are those who say, that you should place foods for the sick which generate inflammation and wind, so that the belly is inflamed, and it is extended, and pushed, the fracture to the exterior.
But we abhor that a cause quick for making a hot abscess should not happen if it does not occur. If it has already happened, then you add it [the medicinal substance] to it.
And some of them said: place a windy mixture on the location, then squeeze it with strength, and it is similar to reasoning. However, it is feared that windy superfluities are attracted to the location due to the arrangement of weakness.
And some of them said: the location should be covered with wool that is already submerged in hot oil: and lice should be placed between the ribs until it is filled up: so that it will be a bandage equal when you turn it according to roundness.
Then the sick person is treated with a pleural treatment from food and medicine.
If something severe presses the sick person, which he cannot bear, and it touches the wound with a sharp object or prick it with a puncture, and we fear above the sick one: then it is necessary that we cut above the location and expose the broken rib. Then we place on it an instrument that conserves sifac, whose memory has preceded: and we cut the bone with ease, and cut it itself.
Then we bring together two lips of the wound, if it is large with stitching: and we treat it itself with plasters until it heals.
If a hot abscess happens meanwhile, then hurry, and pour lice into rose oil, and place it on the location: and the sick person is treated with that which is suitable for abscess.
The patient should also lie on the opposite side until he recovers.
On the restoration of a fracture of the spine and neck.
Fracture sometimes occurs in the bones of the neck, and it rarely happens to them attrition; and similarly with the spondylus of the back. When such a thing happens to someone, and one wishes to know whether it will heal or not: then consider. For if you see his hands already softened, stunned, and dead; and he cannot move them, nor extend them, nor contract them; and when you squeeze them with your fingers, and prick them with a needle, it does not feel this, nor does it feel pain in them: then know that it will not heal, and in many dispositions it is mortal.
If it moves them and feels compression with fingers and prick: then know that the neck bone is intact, and that a broken one will heal with treatment.
If such a thing happens to the spondylus of the back, and one wishes to know whether it will heal or not: then consider his feet; then if you see that they have become softened, and this happens in them as it happened in the hands; furthermore, when he lies on his back, discharge of wind and urine occurs involuntarily; and when he lies on his stomach, discharge of wind and urine occurs involuntarily; and when he wishes to defecate, he cannot: then know that it is mortal. Therefore, do not labor in its treatment.
If nothing happens to him from that, the matter is lighter.
And the treatment for that which happens to him from that is, to apply warm compresses of apothecary's pitch; thus, you should place upon the affected spondylus warm olive oil or fresh cheese of lamb.
Place upon it several times a day until the warm compress is absorbed; then place upon one spot of an ointment that comforts and heals, and bind it over with a bandage, and instruct the sick person to rest quietly and peacefully. He shall not sleep on the part where he found pain, until it is healed.
If it happens to be a fracture of the nasal bone or something that is already separated from it: then one should saw through the skin, and remove it. Then, bring together the two edges of the wound, if it is large, with suturing. Then take care of himself with some fat, from ointments, until he is healed.
If however the last bone of the jaw is broken, and it is at the end of the neck: then one should insert the thumb of the left hand into the nose, and with the other hand, align the broken bone as much as possible, according to what is feasible, and according to the second alignment.
Then place it on top and remove it: and take care of the wound as before, until it is healed.
Chapter on repairing anus.
Chapter 11.
Rarely are the bones of the anus broken. If they break, then the fracture of their bones is not anything but a comminution at their extremities: and they are split lengthwise and inclined towards the inside: and it occurs that there is pain in the place, and puncturing: and the leg which breaks becomes numb.
And the restoration [of the broken bone] is that you place your hand upon it until you stand over the fracture as its shape is.
If the fracture is only at its extremities, then you should adjust that fracture according to how it is prepared for you by the equation, until it resembles its natural shape.
If the fracture is along its length or already inclines towards the inside: then make the weak one lie on his belly, until the restoration of that fracture is prepared for you. When you have adjusted it [the broken bone], place ointment on it; then place a splint made of wood or hide over it, and bind a bandage over the splint where you will not fear the displacement of the fracture or removal of the splint.
And adjust the concavity [of the bone fragments] with what will fill it up until the bandage encloses it according to its equal shape; and command the weak one to sleep on his back or healthy side.
