If a hot abscess happens to him [the patient]: then stop the extension and restoration of it until the hot abscess sits; and place on it whatever sits under it, as before, then return to its restoration; and bind it as necessary.
If it happens in a bone with pus, or if it is crushed, extract it.
Chapter on the restoration of the fracture of the ulna (forearm bone).
The ulna is that which lies between the elbow and the end of the spatula (wrist).
If it breaks, then its restoration is according to one of two modes.
One mode is that you take a flat piece of wood of medium thickness in the shape that follows.
And [the piece of wood] will be bound at its two extremities with two bands.
Then it is suspended from a raised place: and the sick one sits on a seat. Then let him cast his broken arm upon the wood until he reaches its notch, in whose middle is attached a cleft of the wood.
Then raise something heavy above him so that the assistant may stretch him to the lower parts. Then the physician joins the break with his hands until it returns, as required.
And another method is that the sick one lies down on his neck: and hang his hand from his neck with a strap. Then order two assistants that one of them holds what is above the break with his hands: and that the other holds below him, and each one stretches to his side.
And if you wish a stronger extension, then tie below the break with a strap, and above it with another strap: and each of the two assistants stretches to his two sides.
If therefore the break is near the end of the shoulder, then it is necessary that a strap be placed under the notch, and another under the break facing the elbow.
And likewise if the break is near the elbow: then it is necessary that a strap be placed over that place, and over the elbow itself.
Then equal the break with ease and without labor, until when it is joined as required, and applied with a good dressing: then tie it if no hot abscess occurs.
If, however, a hot abscess occurs, then release the binding for seven days. And place above him linen cloth soaked in vinegar and rose oil until the abscess subsides: then tie it: and the method of binding is that you put a plaster over the break.
Then place a new cloth layer on top of the plaster. Then gather the arm upon the support itself, and place his open hand upon his shoulder.
Place the cloth and tie the arm to the support so that the arm remains in place like a star; if it does not prevent you from doing anything or if something from the broken bone does not change on you.
If you fear that something from the broken bone may change on you, then use stars; and there is, so that you place upon the fracture itself a star which is wider and stronger than the other stars. And place the width of each star at the size of a finger; and let the length of the stars be according to the fracture with an addition of three fingers from every part.
Then tie on the stars with the bandage as I said at the beginning of the chapter.
And it is so that your bandage is tighter over the place of the fracture. And as the fracture becomes longer, let the bandage be looser.
If you see the position of the stars and the bandage as we said, in the hour when you restore the member immediately, then do so; and if you fear a hot abscess, then leave off the bandage and the stars until the seventh day, as we said.
Then consider the bandage in all three days to avoid itching or swelling in that place, or for food to be prevented from reaching the member because of the accumulation of superfluity due to the tightness of the bandage. Therefore correct this entire matter as we said.
If you are of the trust that nothing will happen from it: then do not loosen the bandage unless after three days or more.
And let the sick person sleep on his back and place his hands on his stomach; and put under the support a mat full of wool equally.
And consider at every hour of the night or day that the shape of the broken member does not become loose, or the bandage becomes soft. Correct that entirely with your power.
Feed the sick person according to the order we have prescribed, so that the food is subtle at first until the bone begins to close; then it should be increased in size.
For the custom of aid and the leg is that it should be bound for forty days; then it must be loosened, a bath administered, and it should be treated with ointments that correct it.
If the fracture is foul, scrape it; then do not loosen the bandage and splints for fifty days, nor for two months.
Chapter on the restoration of an arm fracture.
The arm is made up of two bones which are called the two humerus bones.
One of them is small: it follows the little finger. And the other is large, and it is placed below the small one from beneath.
Therefore, the larger humerus bone may be broken alone, or the smaller one; and they may break simultaneously.
If therefore the smaller humerus bone is broken at the top, then the restoration of it is easy, and its healing is easier.
And if the larger humerus bone is broken below, the fracture is bad, and its healing is difficult.
It becomes worse when both bones break simultaneously.
If the bone that is broken is the smaller humerus bone at the top, then it is fitting for the doctor at its restoration that he should put an extension of lesser size with ease until it matches it.
But if the larger humerus bone is the one that is broken, then it is necessary to put a strong bandage on it.
And it is necessary to put the hand shape at its restoration and its extension extended over the pulley. The thumb of the hand should be higher than all the fingers; and the inferior joint should be higher than all the digits, or the rest [of them]; and if it is weak, the sitting man should be square with himself, and the pulley be in front of him, or directly below him, at a height so that the weak man does not strain.
Then, the assistant should stretch out the arm from the lower part, either with his hand, or with a rope. And another assistant shall stretch out likewise above [it].
Then the physician shall set the bone until he has reduced it as best as possible to its proper form.
If there are pus-filled sores in the bone fracture, then try to reduce every fragment into its place with your strength.
If it appears that the pus-filled sore is separated and pierces the skin, and there is no hope of restoring it: then puncture it and remove it as we have said in the preceding [instructions].
If there is a bone fracture with a wound: I have already made a separate chapter for that subject, so receive treatment from there.
If a hot abscess occurs at the beginning of your treatment, then prepare a cloth with wax made from rose oil, white beeswax, and have it between thickness and subtlety; and tie the cloth over it with a thin ligature until the abscess settles; then remove the wax, and apply the ready-made plaster prepared from milled powder with egg white. Then place a splint, and let the splint placed over the fracture be somewhat wider and stronger.
And know that the number of splints for the arm is six in various positions; whether it is a fracture in one splint, or in two splints at once.
Then place a stronger and more powerful ligature over the location of the fracture, and apply a ligature with more string than the one at the top or bottom of the fracture; or place a softer ligature a little below the one mentioned earlier, according to what was recalled in the beginning of the chapter.
The fabric that is wrapped around a fracture should be soft, wet and not hard or rough. The thread with which it is tied should be made of linen specifically, neither too fine nor too coarse, as we have said.
After days, examine the limb and ligament. If something happens that requires correction, such as itching in the limb: then, one must heat embrocation for the limb with warm water until the itching stops, and release the unbound limb until it calms down, then repeat the binding.
If the binding has already been softened and the bone removed, along with similar cases: then correct it completely with your skill, and examine again.
If nutrition is prevented from reaching the limb due to excessive binding, then one must soften it slightly and release it for a few days until it reaches the same nutrition, then bind it again.
If nothing happens to the weak limb from what we have said, then it is not necessary to be released, unless after 20 days or about this time.
Then, the weak hand should be suspended to its neck. Its neck should be kept straight and it should be guarded with your skill against excessive movements. Make him sleep on his back.
Know that this fracture is restored within 30 or 40 days; it may possibly be restored in 28 days. Everything is according to the dispositions of temperaments and the dispositions of virtue.
Chapter on the Restoration of Fractures: Hand.
Know that a scissor's hand and the knots of fingers rarely occur a fracture. It occurs to him only through frequent rubbing.
When a break or injury occurs in the foot, then it is necessary that the sick person sits on a level surface opposite to him. Then place his hand upon it extended.
Then the assistant extends the broken bones, and the doctor adjusts them until they join together well.
Then it is necessary to place the plaster and the wool, unless a warm abscess occurs. Then place the splint over it according to the size of the place; and now you have already wrapped it in linen cloth.
If the break is at the lower part near the sole of the foot, then make something similar to a ball from cloth; and order the sick person to bend over it with his injured foot; then wrap it tightly with a long cloth.
And let the splint be of leather, which has softness, so that it may be applied to the body of the foot; and tie it as necessary.
If the break is at the outside, then it is necessary to place a splint over it and another below on the foot; and keep the hand open straight. Then apply the bandage, as the hand rolls; and extend between the fingers with a ligament.
If there is a break in any of the knuckles of the fingers, and it is the thumb, then adjust it as necessary. Then tie it with the palm of the hand; and if there is a wound, place a straight splint below it, small, so that the break is elevated and not moved.
If the break is in the other fingers, such as the middle finger, index finger, or little finger, then adjust it, and tie it to the healthy finger that follows it; or tie all of them according to their continuity; and this is better. Or place a small splint over it, as was said for the thumb.
Consider in your restoration, and after its arrangement remove the tumor with warm [substance]: and apply to it what is necessary, when something happens from that, according to his story's repetition.
On the Restoration of the Hip Fracture.
The hip is often broken and becomes apparent to the senses because it turns towards the inner and posterior part.
And its restoration is done in such a way that the ligament above the fracture is tightened, and another ligament below the fracture. The patient stretches himself out on his face.
Then, stretch every ligament that belongs to its part equally: and this when the fracture is in the middle of the bone.
If it is a fracture close to the root of the hip, then let there be a ligament towards the wing-shaped part, so that the extension falls upward; and another ligament below the fracture.
And similarly if it is a fracture close to the knee, then let there be a ligament near the knee, so that the extension is towards the lower parts.
Then the physician reduces the bone with both hands until he restores it to its natural shape; and the bone is united by a good union.
It is then necessary to apply a plaster and a bandage if there is no tumor on the limb. If a tumor occurs in it: then let it be left for several days until the warm tumor subsides; then return to your treatment.
The bandage of this should be in such a way. It is necessary to wind something hard over the fracture, that is, a bandage; and double it twice or thrice; and there should be excess in it. Then wind the leg until it reaches the calcaneus at the root of the toes.
Insert a long thread between the hip and the leg near the knee towards the lower parts, and let the ends of the thread come from the upper side of each part. Then wind around the leg and hip that which is surplus from the bowels.
Thereafter place a star on the hip at the location of its own fracture, and place one star upon the bone of the leg.
Then fill the voids between the leg and hip with soft cloths, so that they may be made firm.
Thereafter begin the strong suture at the middle above the location of the fracture with three or four turns. And as you extend more with the ligament from the location of the fracture, let your suture be smaller and softer, and looser.
Then direct the two ends of the thread which you inserted between the hip and the leg. Tie then three stars with each of them that are above. Then go with the two ends of the thread to the lower parts until you reach the ankle of the foot. And with each tie also the ends of the stars on the other side: so that the ligament may not be removed from its place.
Then release the suture above that, until it remains, so that no rot can occur on the limb, nor abscess, nor swelling, or similar things. If anything happens from that. Approach then with the solution of it, and correct completely what happened there, according to what we have said many times.
But if there is a splinter in the bone that pierces: then it is necessary, to equalize it, if it is possible for you. And if not, seal over it, and remove it: and treat with care the one who preceded it in memory, until it is healed.
And sometimes this hip fracture is sutured, besides the addition of stars, as we said in assistance and in the arm.
In the restoration of such a fracture, it does not happen that the infirm (person) limps. If only one is restored (the broken part), except if another leg is applied to it, then it is necessary that the one whose it is limps forever.
And know that the hip is bound for 50 days, or a little more, or a little less; all of this is according to differences in complexions and other dispositions.
Chapter on the restoration of the knee joint fracture.
Know that the fracture of the patella of the knee joint occurs rarely, but it often happens that it wears down (rubs).
If there is a fracture, then it is not anything other than a crack or a hole in its parts. And it is with (accompanied by) a wound, and without a wound. It stands on the whole of it with feeling.
The restoration of it is such that it is fitted together from its parts with the fingers until it is united, according to what is possible from fitting and ease, and construction.
Then apply a plaster made from rice flour or milled wheat with egg white; place on top of it a round bandage if needed; and bind above with a suitable binding for that.
Then consider all the dispositions that we have mentioned in other fractures; such as a hot abscess and similar things; so that you oppose to each event with what is suitable for it until it heals.
Chapter on the restoration of the leg fracture.
Know that there are two bones in the leg, one of which is large and is called by the name "leg"; and the other small and slender, and is called "shin".
And it happens that there are the same kinds of fractures for both bones of the leg as for the two bones of the arm; and because of this, the restoration of it is the same as the restoration of the arm, and the operation is one.
If two bones are broken together, the leg is turned in all directions.
If a bone is broken more delicately, the leg is turned to the front part.
If a bone is broken coarsely, you will find it lower and it belongs to those that are not hidden from you.
Then administer the extension, the equality, and the binding of stars according to that equally.
Nevertheless, if the fracture of the leg is a foul one, having many seithages: let the extension be less and the alluviation softer by you.
And in the leg there is something from the operation that resembles the arm and it is, when you equalize the stars, and complete your entire operation, then take two bands made of pine wood which are used for the construction of solaria placed under the tables; they are made of spatulas of the palm and choose those with little thickness and not from the small ones; and let their length be according to the length of the leg from the knee downwards.
Then roll up on one band with two folds according to its length. And place one part of the leg on one side and the other part on the other; they should be from the knee down to the foot.
Then bind the two bands at three places from two ends and the middle. For if pressure is forbidden, the leg inclines to the right and left, and stands firmly in a good position.
And already the cradle made of wood according to the length of the leg should be placed under it to keep it still.
And indeed you should make that in the fracture when there is a wound properly.
Then consider the leg every day and strive with it the utmost diligence from swelling, or inflammation, or other things. If something from these occurs, then oppose it with what is necessary, until it heals, if God wills.
On the Fracture of Bones in the Foot and Fingers.
The calcaneus does not suffer a complete fracture.
Bones of the foot do sometimes suffer a fracture, and fractures do also occur seldom in the fingers; they do not occur unless there is rubbing in various positions.
If a fracture of the bones in the foot occurs and you see those bones rising above one another, then place the injured foot on the ground prepared like when walking.
Then stand you and place your foot on what rises from those bones; then press, or trample it; and apply equal pressure until it returns to its original position.
Then place ointment and wool on top of it; and place a small board under the heel, with two flat heads.
Then tie the foot with a suitable binding when you have wrapped it with linen and whatever else is required.
When three or four days have passed, then loosen the binding. For you will find the bones to be aligned if they are fractured or dislocated. Therefore, the operation is as you see it.
If some fingers are fractured, then restore them and align them according to what I told you in the restoration of fingers of the hand. Then place a star under the finger according to its length, and make it slightly wider; then place this board that I told you about under the foot and bind it with a suitable binding.
If what breaks from the bones is two or three or more, then place a star of its size on each finger, rolled up in linen; and bind the board at the bottom of the foot; and let the standing board be above one head of the foot to hold it well by retention.
It is suitable for you to remember that you should consider all the things I said to you regarding other fractures due to accidents, and apply it to every accident as necessary.
On female facial fracture, clavicle fracture, and the bone of the male thigh.
When a woman's face is fractured, then make her sit upright. Then tilt her head towards the back a little bit, and hold it in that position.
Then the midwife should fill the woman's wound with linen until it is filled, and her face should become round like a sphere from that.
Then shake the woman gently and press her back gradually. That linen will come out until it becomes before the mouth of the wound like a sphere, and the broken bone recovers.
Then place a poultice on her back. Whenever you want to apply the poultice, remove the linen easily, then apply it; bring it back as you initially inserted it, and then return to the original poultice.
Let it be done for seven days or approximately, because the bone recovers itself.
And if you wish, take the bladder of an egg, and tie a reed tube around its opening, and insert the entire bladder into her wound. Then blow air into the tube with force until the bladder inflates inside the wound, because the fracture recovers.
Then fill it again with linen and let it stay for some days as we said before, until it heals.
When the clavicle of a man or woman is fractured, then apply treatment for its restoration and perform what we described regarding the bone of Anchus.
And it is not concealed (the straight line) in these nearby fractures that are not serious, especially in one who has some study or practice.
Understand my book much, for the fracture itself signifies to you in many dispositions a straight way in its restoration and constriction: understand therefore.
In the man's member when it breaks, take the goose's gullet; and insert the rod into it. Then place upon him a cover made of cloth, and wrap it around, and let it be for three days, or about those, and it will heal if God wills.
[Chapter] On the restoration of a bone fracture when it is with a wound.
It is suitable for him who has a fracture with a wound, especially if it is large, such as the thigh bone or arm bone, or similar to it, that you hasten, and have the blood-letting done immediately; if the conditions of blood-letting are attested to you, as we have previously stated.
If the wound discharges blood, then it is necessary that you hasten to the incision of it, so that you powder over it some sieved vinegar; if there is no other present for you.
Then begin to restore the fracture on that same day: and do not delay it, unless an abscess occurs.
If however an abscess hot occurs, then let the restoration of it wait until the ninth day, until the abscess settles down. And do not approach him on the third and fourth day at all, for you would cause harm to come upon him.
If the broken bone projects above the skin and is detected, then it is necessary that you compress it back and level it with your hands with ease and slight extension.
If however the extension is not prepared, and its equality with your hands: then reduce it with this instrument. And the instrument is made of iron, whose length is seven or eight fingers' breadth, and its width according to the size of the wound. And therefore it is necessary for the physician to take from them three or four according to the quantity that is needed for that cure in every type of fracture.
And it should be round and have a small thickness so as not to multiply on the fracture during the hour of surgery, and it should have sharp edges with a margin at its extremities. And let the upper part of it be thick from the middle downwards.
And this is its shape.
It is necessary in this operation that its sharp edge should be bent over the projecting bone's extremity and push it with it once until the bone returns and becomes equal to some measure.
Therefore strive to match the fracture's extremities against each other.
If the fracture's extremity is subtle and the instrument does not receive it well, then it is necessary to cut the bone's extremity until the instrument has control over it.
But if you cannot reduce them with it as we said entirely, then cut the bone with one of the saws that we mentioned, or cut through with a chisel in any way possible for you.
Then smooth what remains on the bone due to roughness and small cortices.
When the bone is reduced and an unusually strong pain is found after its reduction, then you will know that the bone has not returned to its natural place.
Make that. For you give great help to the weak one when you complete the restoration you make of the bone.
Then dip cloth in strong wine, black. And it should properly be in summer. And do not place upon the wound any lead or anything containing oil; lest it cause rot and corruption at the wound site.
Then administer the stars in the hour of completion of your bone restoration. And leave the wound exposed: so that you cut with forceps a hole in the layers according to the size of the wound.
And be careful not to stitch the wound along with the fracture. For often have foolish doctors done this; and it has caused death, or rot, or gangrene, that is, pus, upon their patients. And let your suture be soft, gentle, diverse from the sutures of other fractures.
If the wound is bad, or the wound is large, and you fear some unfavorable occurrences that we have mentioned, and you find pain at the site: then it is not necessary to place stars upon it. And make layers with stiff cloths in place of the stars and stitch it with them.
But if it is a day or two later, and you see the wound beginning to generate pus: then remove from above it the cloth that you placed upon it with the wine. Then administer linen and unguents with which we are accustomed to treat wounds; such as the tetrapharmacon ointment and similar ones.
And it is suitable for you to loosen the binding. And examine the wound every day at morning and evening until it becomes consolidated and healed.
It is necessary that the limb be prepared so that pus runs out of it easily towards the lower parts.
If days pass over the wound and it does not heal nor discharges pus from it: then you know that there are small fractures of bones there. You should examine the wound with a probe. Count how many of those fractures are separated, remove them and extract them. And those which are not separated, and pierce the limb, and cause pain, try to cut them and remove them in every possible way for you.
If it happens that the wound becomes blighted, or ulcerated, i.e., it becomes gangrenous, or of another kind of corruption and putrefaction: then you should apply to each of them what is suitable for it according to the treatment that has been narrated in its own chapter.
And from that which my speech should remain firm and the thought you should have when a bone breaks and protrudes above the limb, such as the hipbone or the arm, and similar ones from large members, it is that you should not presume to draw it, for often death happens from this; on the contrary, leave it alone until it putrefies.
Perhaps it will fall out by itself after twenty or thirty days.
Then take care of the wound if it seems to you; otherwise, leave it alone.
Chapter on the treatment of a swelling at the site of a broken bone.
This swelling often occurs at the site of healing of a broken bone. Mostly, it is near joints; and becomes corrupt in its shape from it. And perhaps it prevents the limb from operating naturally.
Therefore consider if the swelling is recent; administer to it medications that have astringency, such as aloes, olibanum, myrrh, resin of turpentine, acacia, and similar ones.
Take some or all of these [items], and sew them up with sticky wine, or with egg white, or with vinegar; and place them on the swelling in a linen cloth, and tightly bind them there with a good binding.
And release the binding, do not loosen it for many days. Then loosen it, and apply another one until the swelling is removed.
Or bind it over the lead plate properly. For lead has the property of removing all that is feared from limbs.
If the swelling has already turned into a stone and is bound and urgently requires removal: then seal over it from above and cut off any projecting excess; or file it with certain rasps until it is removed; and take care of it until it heals.
Chapter on the treatment of a limb that remains tender after healing.
When a bone fracture heals and the limb remains tender and weak, this is not done except for many reasons. One of the reasons is an abundance of loosening and binding of the ligament that is not as required; or because of the excessive tightness of the ligaments, preventing nutrients from reaching the limb; or because of an excess of embrocation at an incorrect time; or because of a scarcity of blood in the body of the weak person and its debilitation.
And the treatment for this is to feed the weak person, and make his body soft until blood multiplies in him. And administer a bath. And introduce joy and happiness over him.
Then place pitch on the limb, so that the pitch attracts nourishment in large amounts. And continuously apply embrocation to it with warm water until the nourishment returns, and it regains its natural shape.
On the treatment of fractured bones when they are restored crookedly.
When, after the healing of a limb that has been restored, crookedness or protrusion of the fractured bone, or nodosity occurs and the limb becomes deformed due to it, nevertheless, the function of the limb is not prohibited; then it is not appropriate to listen to the opinion of one who suggests breaking the limb again.
And there have been many foolish doctors and restorers who have done this in our land.
This operation is highly blameworthy, leading to death or great fear that it might be fatal.
If the limp and nodosity are recent: then it is necessary to bathe it with warm water in which softening herbs such as althea leaves, its root, melilotus, and similar ones have been boiled. And prepare an ointment for it with a softening plaster such as well-made diachylon.
Or take the mucilage of the althea root and grind it with chicken fat or sesame oil; and apply an ointment with it.
Or take figs that are fatty, pound them with dove excrement and similar ones that are called medicine reducing consolidation.
And sometimes the nodosity is dissolved by persistent, gentle, old-fashioned friction, which is done with wet hands. And the movement of the limb should be applied to every part daily.
If crookedness has already occurred beforehand, and it has become strong and turned into a stone, and the necessity of treating it with iron has arisen: then it is necessary to saw above it, to separate the continuity of the bone, and cut off what protrudes from nodosity or bone with finely sharp tools; and apply ease to it with diligence and care.
On Speech in Dislocation.
Dislocation is the displacement of some joints from their place. Why it should be forbidden from moving and mutilated in appearance of the member. And causes the occurrence of pains on the weak and severe injuries.
When dislocation occurs to someone, then it is necessary to hurry for its restoration; it should not be delayed at all.
For if it is delayed, the location becomes putrid, and perhaps with it the reduction of dislocation.
Therefore, it should not be moved nor extended at the hour of its putrefaction. Because it often causes a spasm and painful injuries.
However, when this happens, then it is necessary to hurry to the bloodletting of the sick person. Then it is let go until the putrefaction ceases.
Then anoint the member with warm water and oil. Then reduce it gently.
And treat every member along with it when its memory comes back to its place.
I myself have already arranged the chapters on dislocation, even according to what preceded in fracture, from the upper part of the body down to the lower part of it.
Chapter on the care of dislocated lower jaw.
The two jaws are rarely dislocated to a lesser extent.
The dislocation of both [occurs] according to one of two modes. Moreover, they are moved away from their places by slight removal and made somewhat soft. Moreover, they are dislocated entirely and completely until they become soft to the chest part, until the saliva of the sick runs, and it cannot hold it or close its mouth, and its tongue is wrapped with speech.
But when the dislocation is slight, then it returns by itself in various ways with a lack of abundance.
But if the dislocation is complete and whole: then it is necessary that its reduction is administered quickly, and it should not be delayed deeply; and there is [the need] for the minister to hold the head of the sick one; and the physician should introduce his thumb of one hand into the root of the Mandible dislocation if the dislocation is from one part. Or he should insert two thumbs at once, if the dislocation is from both parts. And the remaining fingers of his hands should be outside, as required.
And command the sick one to clean his Mandibles and purify them for eating in every respect. The physician should press and push the Mandible until it returns to its place.
If the reduction of it is difficult, especially if both Mandibles are at the same time: then administer steam with hot water and oil until the reduction of both becomes easy; do not delay the reduction of both entirely, as we have said.
When they return and meet and the mouth of the sick one is closed, and they are not made soft: then place on them cotton wool pads, with a preparation made from beeswax; and with rose oil.
Then it should be tied easily with soft cords.
And let the sleep of the sick one be upon his back: and his head should stand between two pillows, so as not to move it to the right and left: neither let him try or be forced to chew something: rather, let his food be made soft, until when the pains are removed, and the jaw is tied: then let him eat whatever he wants, and let that be given to him with ease.
He should not labor on the operation of his mouth while eating and drinking, and speaking, until the jaw is tied, and it heals.
If the reduction of both jaws becomes difficult when they dislocate at some hour and do not return to their places: then often fever occurs, and there is constant soda: and perhaps the stomach of the sick one breaks open, and he vomits pure bile.
Therefore when you see that: then know that it is a loss, and the one to whom that happens often dies within ten days.
Section on the reduction of dislocation of the jaw and the end of the arm.
The collarbone is not dislocated from inside outward due to its continuity with the chest. It becomes dislocated sometimes outside, and this is perceived by feeling.
And the restoration of it is that the sick one lies on his back, and extends his arms. Then, with your bent hand, press the dislocated joint, because it will return.
Then place an ointment on it, and pack it with cotton, and bind it.
However, the extremities that follow the elbow, which are continuous with it, do not become dislocated unless rarely. If it becomes dislocated on any day: then it must be reduced and set according to what has been said, and what you have prepared for yourself.
Then place upon it [the wound] the plaster, and the leeches, and the ligature. And instruct the sick man to adhere to tranquility and rest until he is healed.
When this same cure brings the edge of the arm back to its place, it should also be separated from its place.
Chapter on the reduction of dislocation of the arm.
Know that the humerus is not dislocated, unless in three ways.
One of them is, it dislocates to the part of the pectoral at the lower side. The second is, it dislocates to the chest part. And perhaps it dislocates to the upper part of the humerus: and that happens rarely.
Nor is it dislocated at the backside, because of the spade [splint]. Nor is it dislocated to the front side, because of the nerves.
Indeed, it is most often dislocated and comes out to the lower side turned towards the pectoral: especially in those whose flesh is scanty. Because it goes out quickly in them and enters quickly.
But in those whose flesh is abundant, the reverse holds true: namely, because it goes out with difficulty and enters with difficulty.
And perhaps some people experience a blow or an accident and the humerus is dislocated by a hot application, and it is thought to be so because it has already been dislocated.
Therefore, one must experiment with that until you are certain of its nature, and then proceed in treating it.
And the dislocation is known when it is to the lower side turned towards the pectoral, and you shall distinguish between a dislocated humerus and a healthy humerus. For you will find among them an evident difference, and in the head of the humerus there is a hollow, and beneath the pectoral at the touch of the head of the humerus as if it were an egg: and an invalid cannot bring his hand to his ear nor move it with all movements.
And similarly, if it is dislocated towards the chest or upward: then you will find an evident touch, and it cannot be hidden.
And this kind of dislocation has an easy reduction when it is recent or the child is weak.
The reduction is that he elevates his hand to above. Then place your thumbs beneath his, and with strength lift the joint until it is in its place; then he will elevate his hand, and stretch it upward. Then put it down, for he quickly returns.
If it does not return as mentioned, and from the beginning when dislocation occurs are many days: then it is necessary that the sick one bathe in warm water, and receive embrocation which softens and soothes, just as if they decoct root of althea, and fenugreek, and melilotus in water, and give it.
Then the sick one lies on his back upon his back; and let a sphere and wool be placed under his thumb which is equal in softness and tightness.
Then the doctor places his heel upon the sphere, and lifts the head of the humerus with force, and draws the hand of the sick one to below.
And if you wish, reduce him according to this method; and it is that you make the man present who is taller than the sick one, and stands on his side, and introduces his humerus under the thumb of the sick one: and lifts the thumb of the sick one to above, until the sick one is suspended in the air. And another person pulls the hand of the sick one to below his stomach. If the sick one is light: then it is necessary that he be suspended with something else, in order to weigh him down; for the dislocation quickly returns.
And it is also reduced according to another method; and it is that a long wooden rod is fashioned on the ground, whose head is rounded in form: like a mitten of a hammer-maker; which is neither too heavy nor too thin. Then place it under the patient when logs of soft cloth are placed on his head, and let the patient stand according to the length of the logs.
Then extend his hands towards the lower part from the other side, and extend his body from the other side with force, for the joint will return to its place quickly.
If the reduction (restoration) is difficult by all that has been said, then use this care: and it is to take a log whose length is the size of two forearms and its width four fingers, and its thickness two fingers, with rounded head to widen its entrance into the depth for pressing, according to this shape.
Then wrap around his head with soft cloth logs, so as not to injure the log supporting the patient.
Then place it under the support of the patient, so that his hand or arm may be extended over the log to below, and tie the log above the arm and hand and lower limb.
Then place the arm on a step of the ladder widthwise, and extend the hand to below, and let the rest of the body hang suspended from the other side. For the joint enters immediately.
Since the entrance is filled to the greatest extent possible, then it is necessary that you place under the support a sphere made of wool equal in size to it. Then place a prepared plaster made from milled flour with balm and egg white on the entire shoulder as it is rolled on top of it.
Then tie a suitable suture below the support and roll the bandage over the plaster above it, connect the patient's hand to his neck and do not move his hand for seven days.
It is necessary that a scanty food be placed (for the patient) until the limb recovers its strength, since it heals more quickly in this way.
Then it should be placed larger after seven days, or five, and it should be opened with motion. If therefore it is firm and does not become soft, then it is healed.
If however the union is dislocated many times on account of the moisture that occurs to it or on account of some other cause: then it is necessary that a cautery be administered in it with three holes, according to what has been said in the chapter on cautery.
If this is done entirely, and the ligament is loosened after seven days, and the union does not become firm, and a plaster and pressure are applied to it several times, and it does not become firm and falls, and cannot be raised upwards: then you know that the nerves which are to the head of the humerus have been cut or extended or softened: and you know that the union will never fuse in its place.
Dislocation that happens towards the chest and breast, or to the back reduction, is made with pressure and extension with hands until it returns, and a remaining pressure and care are administered until it heals.
If after healing there is heaviness in the member and slowness of its motion, then the sick person should use baths many times until that heaviness is relieved, and it returns to its first nature.
Chapter on the treatment of shoulder dislocation.
You know that the union of the shoulder dislocates with difficulty and similarly returns with difficulty; and it is indeed dislocated to all parts, but especially in front or behind.
And the dislocation of the shoulder is not hidden from you; because it falls under sight and touch, according to whatever form it is dislocated. And when you join a dislocated shoulder with the healthy one, that is clearly shown to you. The joint must be closed, and it cannot be doubled over the arm nor touch its own shoulder.
You should rush and immediately reduce a dislocation before it gets an abscess.
For if an abscess occurs, the reduction becomes difficult; and it may not heal at all, especially when it is towards the back. Indeed, every kind of dislocation is worse and more painful; and death often accompanies it.
And its restoration, when it can be reduced, is to extend the hand with two hands; and the arm should be extended, and the hands of the physician are above and below the elbow, and he himself pushes the joint with his thumbs or the root of his palm until it returns to its place.
Or the dislocation is towards the front: then it is reduced in such a way that the hand doubles over with its own hand until it strikes the elbow which is dislocated with the root of its palm.
If the dislocation does not obey reduction, then administer a violent extension, very strong; and it is to be done that two attendants stretch the arm; and they are to hold it so that it is not moved during the extension.
Then the arm is rotated to all parts after it has been wrapped around the hand with a double cloth or wide bandage.
And when the physician touches the joint with his hand, he binds it with oil; so that it is one of those things which help in the lubricity of the joint with ease. Then he pushes the joint forcefully until it returns.
After its reduction, it is necessary to place an ointment on it, which contains styptic and purifying properties with egg white; and it is to be bandaged tightly. And let the arm of the sick person be suspended from the neck for some days.
Then it is loosened. If the joint becomes fixed in its place, then loosen the bandage from it and let it be.
And if you see that the joint is not well closed, then apply the plaster again, and bandage it and let it be for some days until it is closed. Then loosen it.
If, however, there is pain after the reduction of the joint and it has movement: then administer humectation in a bath, fine friction, and compression until it is lined up, or place upon the joint, that is, the fat of the ram's woolen cloth. Then let it be bound and kept for a day and a night over it. Then remove it, and immerse it in the bath. And when it sweats, then friction the joint with a moderate friction. Then apply to it three times with warm poultices when entering the bath until it is softened.
And if you wish, place upon it dried dung, warm, mixed with butter, and bind over it.
Make this often. It becomes softened, and returns to its former condition.
Chapter on the treatment of dislocations by Albucasis.
The hand of Almahassem is frequently dislocated, that is, the joint which is between the arm and the hand.
And the reduction of this dislocation is easy. On the contrary, the other joints are more difficult to reduce. However, speed must be taken in the reduction of this dislocation within the hour in which it is dislocated, before the place is loosened: or a hot abscess occurs at the site.
Reduction of dislocation is to place Mahassem's weak hand on a table, and let the attendant extend it; and the physician shall place his palm on the prominent joint, and push until it returns.
Furthermore, it is to be considered if the palm has already retreated into the body of the hand: then let the weak place his back of the hand on a table for extension and reduction; and if the dislocation is proceeding towards the back of the hand, then let the position of the hand be placed intrinsically on the table, that is, the inner part of the hand should be placed on the table, so that the physician may fall upon the prominent joint.
If it returns immediately, that is good; and if not, then bind it with a plaster sitting on the abscess; and leave it alone, do not repeat that.
For if it cannot bear (the pressure), nor can you perform reduction of it afterwards when the days pass, but the joint remains according to its twist; and nothing harms the weak unless what is softened for the hand, and nothing can be bound; then know that the nerves have been cut or rubbed.
Therefore, there is no skill in it except to bind with a cautery near to its destruction. And perhaps it does not bring anything about.
When Almahassem does not return, then place an ointment on him as we have said. Then bind it, and let it be for five days; afterwards it is to be loosened and let go, and exercise the hand.
If its movement is taken away, and something heavy happens in it: then soften it with warm water, and rub it gently many times until it is softened.
Chapter on the cure of finger dislocations.
Chapter XXX: When a finger is dislocated from any part, or from the back of the palm to its body, or the inside of it: then extend the finger and push the dislocation with your thumbs until it returns.
Then support the head of the digit, and suspend it towards the side where it is dislocated, and leave it for two days.
Then extend it until it aligns with its proper position on that day.
When it is night, tie it according to the story given: and do not cease to loosen it during the day: and exercise it with movement: and tie it at night.
Do this for several days until it tightens.
And do the same with it if it is dislocated towards the palm of the hand: and tie it to that side, and perform your first operation on it until it heals.
And do the same with it when it is dislocated towards other parts.
Chapter on the treatment of dislocation of the spondylus of the back.
When a person experiences dislocation of the spine, either complete of the neck, or multiple spondyles are displaced from their places: then there is no cure in it, since death approaches the weak.
And the sign of this is that the hunger of the weak escapes without will, and he cannot hold it; and many times some members of his are softened, or both legs of him, or both arms of himself, or one of them; or a spondyle is displaced from its place.
For many times it is displaced, which is often done to four parts.
That which is displaced towards the rear is called a humpback.
The cure for it is to consider whether the humpback occurs from childhood; then there is no cure in it, and there is no healing at all from it.
But in that which occurs from an accident, or blow, or similar things, the ancients have already said the methods of treatment in a lengthy speech. Of this, more often than not it does not return with recovery.
I myself have already abbreviated from that which is rarely justified by a large part due to my proximity to the subject: and in my explanation, I explained that, and shaped its instrument in a different way from how they showed it and explained it.
Therefore, I say that in humpback which occurs from the front part, there is no talent, nor healing from it.
And similarly in that which occurs to both sides.
And it is not cured from them, unless that which occurs in the back properly, with what I speak: and it is to extend the weak over his face on an equal plank nearly up to the wall; and expand under him a wet cloth, so as not to harm his chest.
Then place a straight stick fixed in the bowl on the ground, at the end of the foot, about its head: and another stick about its feet at the other end of the foot: and let a servant hold the stick, and it shall not be fixed in the bowl. And let another servant hold the other one.
Then bend over the head of the patient, and under his chin tie a firm bandage, and extend the end of the bandage to the stick that is at his head, and tie it there.
Then bind another bandage above the ankles of the patient, and at the joints of his knees. Then gather all the bandages together, and tie them to the other stick that is at his feet.
Then let all the servants extend their bandages to the part of the stick, and neither of the sticks should be moved from its place where it is fixed: but they shall not be fastened, as we have said.
And the doctor shall put his hands on the hip-bone with force, until he returns: or place a board over it. Then he attaches his feet to the board until he returns.
If he does not return through this treatment, then receive a board whose length is about three arms. And carve a hole in the wall that we mentioned which is near the patient, into which the end of the board may enter. Then place the middle of the board on the hip-bone. And let the doctor place his feet on the other end. And bind tightly, until the hip-bone is dislocated, and he returns to his place.
Then, if you wish, operate with a lathe that turns by hand: and it is so that you may carve two sticks in the ground at the head of the patient's last foot, whose length is that of an arm: and at his head at the last foot two sticks.
And let the length be that which is between every stick and a palm [width]: and now a hole has been made in every place where a tourniquet runs: and let all the sticks be fixed strongly to the ground so that they do not move at all.
And insert a round stick: and there is a tourniquet in which a cord is turned in the holes of two sticks.
And at the end of it there is a hole where the stick is fixed, whose length is a palm plus what is turned: and similarly in two other sticks.
Then bind the cords which you have bound around the chest of the sick man at the tourniquet that is near his head: and bind the cords which you have bound around his legs at the tourniquet that is near his feet.
Then stand by every tourniquet the servant who turns his hand with a cloth, with which the tourniquet is turned: and the physician measures the pulse, as we have said.
This is the form of the tourniquet that follows.
Then when the pulse has been measured and the place is ready, then it is necessary to put on a plaster made from excrement and egg white. Then apply [a substance called] stuppa. Then put on top of the plaster a star-shaped piece from wood, whose width is that of three fingers, or about those [dimensions]. And its length shall be the quantity that covers the depression and the healthy part of the pulse.
Then bind it with the cord we have mentioned. And the sick man shall use nourishment that is subtle until he is well.
If there remains something raised in the place at the end of healing: then it is necessary to administer medicines that soften and soothe, with the use of the tablet we have mentioned for a long time. And now, it is administered on that plate of lead.
Occasionally, a protrusion happens in the last vertebra of the back; it is thought to be a dislocation, and that bone which has become prominent is involved. Therefore, it is not proper to presume this medicine for him; perhaps it will cause death.
Chapter on the treatment of a dislocated ankle.
Know that the union of the ankle and shoulder joint does not occur except in a dislocation; nor does it happen to them what happens to other joints from a slight displacement and hollowing out.
Indeed, the ankle joint is dislocated according to four parts. Because it is dislocated toward the front and toward the back.
It is indeed dislocated most of all to the inside; it is dislocated less frequently to the front and to the back.
The treatment of a dislocation to the inside is as follows: because when you join the limb of a sick person with a healthy one, it is longer; and the knee joint of the sick person is more prominent than that of a healthy one, and the sick person cannot double his foot near the knee; and there is a place following the knee which is hollowed out by a hollow instrument, because the head of the hip has already reached that place.
The sign of one who has a dislocation to the outside is that his symptoms are opposite these symptoms.
The sign of one who has a dislocation to the front is that he spreads out the healthy leg to its full extent; however, he cannot double it without pain in the knee, and if he attempts to walk, he cannot step forward on it; and urine retention occurs in him, his knee becomes hollowed out, and during walking there is a striking of his foot on the heel.
The sign of the one who experiences dislocation at the posterior [region] is that it does not extend the knee, nor can he double it before doubling the thigh; and his leg is also shortened on the other side; and the thigh is softened, and it has the head of the hip in that place.
The reduction [of this dislocation] is of the following forms: consider then, if the dislocation is old and has already degenerated with it. Then if you administer the reduction of it, and it remains according to its position; then there is no treatment at all in it. Therefore, one should not make a rash assumption about it [the condition].
In the one whose dislocation is recent, and belongs to one of the four forms of dislocations: then go ahead and apply pressure on it, extend it towards the inside and outside, and move it right and left; perhaps it will return, and no other care is needed.
If it does not return in this way, then a strong attendant should prepare and extend the leg from below: either with his hands or with a rope that ties above the knee; and another assistant should extend his hand above, so that he may insert his hands under the armpits of the patient. Then tie a soft rope around the root of the hip and hold the end of another rope.
And the extension [of the leg] should be from the front of the thigh to the neck of the collarbone, or from the back to the backbone.
And the extension [of all] should be one and the same until the sick man with his body is raised from the ground and remains hanging.
This is indeed a common form of the four forms [of dislocation].
If the dislocation returns as we said, it is good; and if not, then it is necessary to follow the specific cure mentioned below.
In the reduction [of a dislocation], when it is toward the inner parts, the sick one must lie on his healthy side. Then put the bandage over the root of the hip, where it is between the head of the hip and the place that is below the groin. Then extend the bandage upward from the groin to the higher part of the body, to the neck (region).
Then another strong man takes hold of his arm and reduces the large place of the weak hip by extending it to the outside with great force. Now it is back in its own place.
And this method is easier than the other methods of treatment, by which this limb is reduced.
If, however, it is dislocated above you and does not obey you at all with this method of treatment, then it is necessary that two weak feet be bound together with a strong bandage, loosely, on both heels, and on both knees: and the length of each one should be four fingers' breadths: and the weak leg should be extended by more than two fingers.
Then the sick one is suspended over the top of a log that is in the house; and its length from the ground should be two forearms' lengths.
Then command a strong youth to bind the head of the hip; and suspend another with the sick one: and another youth pushes, while binding the hip with force, because when it is bound together, it returns quickly to its own place.
In the reduction [of a dislocation], when it is toward the outer parts, the sick one must lie on a couch, as we have said in the one with a hump. And bind the bandage over his weak leg and over his chest.
Then two logs are placed, one at his feet and another at his head. Then place a support stick in the middle of the foot, secure it well; upon which let the wet cloths be wrapped, so as not to hurt the weak; and let the stick be between his thighs, so that he is not drawn downwards during extension.
Then let every attendant go to their place, and the doctor shall adjust the reduction with his hand.
If it obeys in returning, it will be good.
And if not, place it on the table and press it evenly, as we have said in swelling, likewise. However, it is necessary that the weak one lies on the side of the healthy.
And when the dislocation is towards the front, then it should be extended quickly; and this is according to the same narrative on the foot; and the doctor shall place the palm of his right hand on the sick person's thigh. Then bend him with one hand, and with that bending place him extended towards the lower part of the leg.
If the dislocation is towards the back: then it should not be extended downwards; and the sick one should not be raised up from the ground; instead, he should be placed on a hard surface, as it should also be done if his dislocation is towards the outside, according to what we said about his lying on two sides: and it is on his face and his limbs are extended; according to what we said recently.
And it is necessary that compression is administered with a board also on the place from which the joint departs, when the reduction is completed according to what is required.
And the sign of his reduction is not hidden from you; and it is to extend two legs of the sick one; and when you see them even, and the sick person tightens his leg, and expands himself without any resistance; then you will know that the limb has returned, according to what is required: then join the thighs, and apply a plaster, and tie with gauze, that is, roller; in such a way that the dislocation does not move towards any part; and let the sick one remain quiet for three or four days.
Then unfasten the ligament, and apply the plaster, and compare the leg with the other: then if you see that they are equal in position, then you will know that the dislocation is already fixed: therefore leave the patient to walk.
And if you see something softening: then return, and apply the plaster again, and bind it according to your first binding: and let it be for three days.
Then unfasten it and cause it to slow down in walking on both sides, until it recovers well.
Chapter on the Dislocations of the Knee.
The knee is dislocated in three ways. It is dislocated to the outside, to the inside, and to the below (i.e., the back).
And it is not dislocated to the front.
The sign of this dislocation is that you order the patient to apply his leg to his hip. But if it does not stick to the hip: then know, that the knee is dislocated.
And the restoration of all the modes of dislocation is that you make the patient sit upright, so that he now extends his leg if possible over it and presses his back against his face; and place his foot between your feet.
Then cause the knee to stick to your palms, and keep them straight across the knee. Then apply two palms of your hand firmly on both sides of the knee. And let another servant extend his foot, until the knee returns to its place.
The sign of return is that the leg sticks to the hip gently, not forcefully.
Then apply a plaster to it, and cause the leg to stick to the hip. Then bind them together with ointment for three or four days. Then dissolve it.
And let not the sick person walk for some days until he is cured.
If he refuses to return, then give him a strong extension with ligaments, whose description is found in the treatment of an aneurysm, until he returns.
Chapter on the treatment of dislocated heel bone.
Calcaneus is sometimes removed by a slight removal, and sometimes it is dislocated according to its extent. Dislocation of it happens either towards the internal or external parts.
The sign of its dislocation is that you see the calcaneus swollen, projecting towards the part to which it is dislocated.
But the treatment of its removal is easy return: and it is that it is extended with ease with hands, and adjusted until it returns.
However, in its treatment when it is dislocated according to its extent, one must make the patient sit straight: and a strong assistant should hold him very firmly from the back part of his middle.
Then hold with your right hand the foot from above its part, and with your left hand from below the foot at the place of the ankle.
Then pull the foot towards you with your right hand. And with your left hand in two places.
Then extend it with your left and push the sole of the foot towards you with your right without strain.
Do that twice as we have said.
Then push the sole of the foot towards the leg at the third place, and you pull the ankle.
If therefore it returns at one or two places according to this method and you see the foot aligned, it is good: and if not, then repeat the procedure on him; because he will return.
But if the reduction of it with him as we have said is forbidden to you: then make the patient lie on his back on the ground, and fix a peg in the ground firmly so that he falls between his thighs; and now roll a cloth over him so that he does not harm the sick one.
Then receive an assistant his thigh: then another extends the leg, either with hands or with a strap that binds around its neck. Then let every attendant extend himself opposite to the extension of his own [position]: and let the pillow be standing between the two hips of the sick person, holding him, so that his body is not drawn downwards during the extension.
Then the physician should dislocate [the bone] with his hands, and another attendant holds the sound leg towards the lower parts. For dislocation is regained quickly.
When therefore dislocation has returned and the health of its return is shown to you: then put an ointment and a plaster, and sew it firmly with bandages, and bind the foot with a bandage to the leg.
And it is necessary that you be careful or fearful of the nerves which are above the heel at the back, so that the bandage is not too tight and harms them.
Then let him go for two or three days. If the bandage becomes softened, sew it again: then dissolve it on the third or fourth day: and forbid the sick person from walking for forty days. But if he attempts to walk before that time, there will not be safety unless the dislocation is dissolved upon him, and it is spoiled, and it will not receive treatment afterwards.
If it happens to him a hot abscess: then it is necessary that you administer for its sedation what was previously told in another place about the cure, and an embrocation until it is removed.
Chapter on Dislocation of Fingers of the Foot and Cure.
It is necessary that it be equalized what is being dislocated with little extension without effort: and this is not difficult, rather it is easy.
But if the dislocation is in some nodes of the back of the foot: then it is necessary that the sick person sits his foot on an equal place to the ground, or on a table: and he stands thus as walking.
Then you stand, and place your foot upon that which projects from those joints. Then press it with your foot with force until it returns and you see it has become even, and no projection appears in its place.
Then place under the heel of that foot a board which can hold the entire foot, having two handles. Then bind it with a ligature decent three days. Then unbind it and let him rest from walking for many days until it binds and assures against relapse.
On the types of dislocations with wound or fracture.
When something happens to it (the wound or fracture) and you attempt its cure and restoration, often death occurs; and therefore, one should not presume about the cure of such a thing unless he is wise in long practice, merciful, pious, slow, not bold, nor presumptuous; and that he uses for the beginning of treatment only medicines which quiet inflammations hot only; and that he allows the weak to the divine judgment. Unless you see or hope for recovery from destruction with mildness of illness and where something appears with hope. Therefore, attempt the reduction of it immediately at the beginning of the matter, before a hot abscess occurs.
If the limb then returns as we desire, then administer a regimen that quiets the abscess; and cure the wound with it which suits it from dry ointments.
If the dislocation is with a fracture, and pus-filled sores separate from the bone occur, then attempt their removal; and make in it what was said for simple illnesses in the places of those sores.
And take care according to your strength, and guard yourself from the entrance into the way of fear; according to what was previously said to you.
It is better for you with your Creator, and more lasting for your glory, and more healthy for your body with them at your time.
